# The First Fixed Opening

*A highly readable mathematics textbook built from source construction, proof traction, and disciplined symbolic extension.*

> **Working status:** unified flat Markdown scaffold. The detailed chapter scaffold, diagram inventory, curriculum map, and earlier seed prose have been combined into this single document.

---

<!-- BEGIN CLEAN_TOP_MATTER_V1 -->

# Project Orientation

This document is the working scaffold for a readable mathematics textbook.

The book begins with one small action: a compass opening is fixed. From that opening, the construction builds a boundary, stations, steps, cells, a sixfold field, direction families, a triangular field, geometry, ratio, trigonometry, coordinate audit, and algebra.

The main text is not the full source ledger. The main text is the learner-facing path through the mathematics. The complete 615-block source ledger belongs in the appendix as the audit trail.

The reader is not asked to memorize the whole structure. The reader is not asked to trust a claim because it sounds familiar. Each idea should return as something visible: an opening, a sweep, a point, a step, a triangle, a field, a ratio, a coordinate, or a symbol.

The diagrams carry the memory. The words only help the reader notice what the construction has already made.

---

# Introduction - Look First

This book begins with one small action.

Open a compass.

Do not measure a page. Do not draw a grid. Do not write an equation.

Just open the compass and keep the opening fixed.

One leg has the point. One leg has the pencil. The space between them is the opening. Once that opening is chosen, the book asks one question again and again:

What can this fixed opening make?

At first, it makes very little.

The point can stay. The pencil can move. The opening does not change.

Then the pencil sweeps. A boundary appears.

A point on the boundary can be chosen. Now the compass can stand there.

From that new station, the same opening can step to another boundary point.

One step becomes a triangle.

The same step repeats.

After six steps, the path comes back to its start.

The six triangles around the center make a first field. The outside of that field is a hexagon. The sides of the hexagon show three directions. Those directions can continue. Where they continue and meet, more cells appear.

A triangular field grows from the first opening.

That field is where the rest of the book begins to live.

Geometry begins when we read figures the field has already made.

Rightness appears when selected connectors meet cleanly inside the field.

Ratio appears when a right triangle lets us compare its sides.

Trigonometry appears when those ratios receive names.

Coordinates appear later. They do not create the field. They help us record and check it.

Algebra appears later too. It does not invent relations. It remembers relations in symbols and lets them change form without losing their truth.

This is the promise of the book:

You will not be asked to trust a name before the picture has earned it.

You will not be asked to memorize what the diagram can show again.

You will not be asked to accept a formula as if it came from nowhere.

The construction comes first.

The name comes after.

Look first. Name later.

---
# Reader Promise

You do not need to remember everything in advance.

You do not need to trust anything because it has a famous name.

This book will keep bringing the construction back to the page. When a new idea appears, it should appear first as something you can see or draw. A name comes later, after the thing has already become visible.

The rule is simple:

Look first. Name later.

The picture keeps the record. The words only point.

---

# Style Guide and Voice Contract

This book is written for a normal, curious 14-year-old reader.

The voice should be light, clear, and visual. It should not sound like a proof ledger. It should not sound like a lecture in advanced mathematics. It should feel like a calm guide standing beside a diagram.

Writing rules:

1. Say it as early as it can be seen.
2. Say it as simply as it can be said.
3. Put the picture before the name.
4. Put the action before the abstraction.
5. Use one new idea at a time.
6. Keep sentences short when the idea is new.
7. Never ask the reader to memorize what the figure can show again.
8. Never ask the reader to trust what the construction has not yet shown.
9. Do not use proof-machinery language in the main chapters.
10. Keep source references, but do not let them dominate the chapter.

Preferred style:

The compass point stays. The pencil is allowed to move. But the opening does not change.

We choose a point on the boundary. Now the compass can stand there.

Look at the hexagon. Its sides come in three pairs. Each pair goes the same way. Those are our three directions.

Coordinates come after the picture. They help us check what is already there.

Algebra remembers the relation in symbols. It does not invent the relation.

---

# Diagram Policy

The diagrams are not decoration. They are the visual reservoir of the book.

Each diagram should show the easiest available version of the idea. If the hexagon already shows the direction families, show them in the hexagon. If the first triangle already shows equal sides, show the equal sides there. Do not delay an idea when the picture has already made it simple.

Diagram rules:

1. Use the earliest clear picture.
2. Prefer visible action over symbolic explanation.
3. Avoid clutter.
4. Use labels only when they help the reader see.
5. Use the same visual object again when it carries memory.
6. Let the reader return to the diagram instead of memorizing a chain of facts.
7. Show what is earned before naming it formally.

A diagram should answer this question:

What can the reader see here without being asked to trust me?

---

# Mathematical Spine

The main path of the book is:

1. Fixed opening
2. Boundary
3. Boundary station
4. Equal-opening step
5. Equal-opening cell
6. Sixfold closure
7. Central turn field
8. Direction families
9. Triangular field
10. Transport
11. Geometry
12. Rightness
13. Ratio
14. Trigonometry
15. Coordinate audit
16. Symbolic memory
17. Algebraic transformation

This list is not a memory test. It is a map. The chapters should keep returning to the visible construction behind each step.

---

# Chapter Template Contract

Most chapters should follow this shape:

## Chapter N - Title

### What to Look At

The picture or construction the reader should begin with.

### What Moves

The action in the construction.

### What Stays the Same

The relation that survives the action.

### What We Can Now Say

The new word, permission, or mathematical idea that has been earned.

### What We Are Not Saying Yet

The boundary. The thing we are not importing early.

### Source Reference

The affordance, theorem, bridge block, or fuse block that supports the chapter.

This template is a guide, not a cage. Use it to keep the chapter clear. Do not let it make the prose stiff.

<!-- END CLEAN_TOP_MATTER_V1 -->

# Preface - What This Book Is Doing

This book begins with a single mathematical act: a compass opening is fixed.

That act is small enough to hold in your hand.

But it is strong enough to begin a chain.

From the fixed opening, a boundary is swept.

From the boundary, stations are selected.

From stations, steps are carried.

From steps, cells are formed.

From cells, a turn closes.

From the closed turn, direction recurs.

From recurring direction, a field extends.

From the field, geometry, trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and algebra can be read.

The book does not start by saying, "Here are the rules. Memorize them."

It starts by asking you to look.

When a new idea appears, it should appear first as something visible: an opening, a sweep, a point, a step, a triangle, a field, a ratio, a coordinate, or a symbol carrying a relation.

The complete source ledger is preserved in Appendix A. That appendix is the audit trail. It keeps the full record of the mathematical permissions behind the book.

The main chapters do something different.

They give the learner-facing path.

They keep the construction on the page.

They let the diagram do the memory work.

They say only what the reader can see, or what the construction has already earned.

This matters because mathematics is often taught as if the names come first.

Circle.

Triangle.

Parallel.

Perpendicular.

Sine.

Coordinate.

Equation.

But in this book, the name waits until the thing has appeared.

A circle is first a swept boundary.

A triangle is first three stations joined by one opening.

A direction is first two sides that run the same way.

A ratio is first one side compared with another.

A coordinate is first a record placed on a station that already exists.

An equation is first a relation held still.

The path is gentle, but it is not loose.

Every new permission has to come from the construction, or from a clear record of the construction.

The reader is allowed to ask:

Where did that come from?

The book should always be ready to answer:

Look here.

---
# How to Read This Book

Read this book with a pencil nearby.

You do not need special tools for every page, but the book is meant to be drawable. When a construction matters, pause and sketch it. A rough sketch is usually enough.

The chapters follow a steady rhythm.

First, look at the construction.

Then notice what moves.

Then notice what stays the same.

Then say what the picture now allows.

Only after that should a name or symbol appear.

A chapter may use sections such as:

- What to Look At
- What Moves
- What Stays the Same
- What We Can Now Say
- What We Are Not Saying Yet
- Core Permission Earned
- Source Reference

These headings are not meant to make the reading stiff. They are guardrails.

They help keep the order honest.

The picture comes first.

The action comes next.

The permission follows.

The name comes after.

When you see **Core Permission Earned**, treat it as a small door opening. It tells you what the book is now allowed to use later.

For example:

The same compass opening can be used again.

A swept opening makes a boundary.

A selected boundary point can become a station.

One boundary station can step to another by the fixed opening.

Six equal steps close.

Each of those permissions is small. Together they build the field.

When you see **What Is Not Yet Allowed**, do not treat it as a warning that you are behind. It is the opposite. It means the book is protecting you from being rushed.

We do not use coordinates before the field exists.

We do not use sine before the ratio is visible.

We do not use algebra before there is a relation to remember.

The appendices are different from the main chapters.

Appendix A will hold the complete source ledger. That is the audit trail.

Appendix B is reserved for editorial source notes if they are needed later.

Appendix C and Appendix D help track dependencies and source references.

Appendix E and Appendix F help track diagrams.

You do not need to read the appendices before reading the chapters.

The main text is the path.

The appendices are the record behind the path.

A good way to read each chapter is:

1. Find the diagram or imagine it.
2. Ask what action is happening.
3. Ask what stays unchanged.
4. Read the permission the action has earned.
5. Notice what the chapter refuses to import early.

If a sentence feels too abstract, return to the picture.

If a name feels too sudden, ask what the construction has already shown.

If a formula appears later, ask what relation it is remembering.

The book began with one fixed opening.

Keep returning to it.

The opening stays.

The construction grows.

The diagram remembers.

---

# Part I - The First Measure

This part begins with the smallest possible mathematical promise.

The compass opening is fixed.

Nothing else is allowed to carry the book yet. Not a formula. Not a grid. Not a theorem name.

First, the reader sees that the compass point can stay while the pencil moves. Then the reader sees that the whole compass can move while the opening stays the same.

That is the first measure.

It is not a number yet. It is not a coordinate yet. It is a reach that can be used again.

By the end of this part, the fixed opening has swept a boundary, and one point on that boundary has become a new station.

That is enough to begin stepping.

---
## Chapter 1 - The Fixed Opening

### Start Here

Open a compass.

One leg has the sharp point. The other leg has the pencil.

Do not draw a shape yet. Do not measure anything. Just look at the space between the point and the pencil.

That space is the opening.

Now keep it.

The point can stand in one place. The pencil can move. The hand can turn. But the opening does not change.

That is the first fact of the book.

The point may move later. The whole compass may move later. But if the hinge is not changed, the opening stays the same.

So the opening can travel.

This does not give us a circle yet.

It does not give us a triangle yet.

It does not give us coordinates.

It gives only one small permission:

The compass can move without forgetting its opening.

### What to Notice

The compass has two things happening at once.

One thing can move.

One thing can stay the same.

The point can be lifted and placed somewhere else. The pencil point moves with it. But the distance between the two legs remains fixed.

That fixed reach is the first memory of the book.

### Chapter Close

The first lesson is small on purpose.

We have not made a shape yet.

We have not named a theorem.

We have only learned that one opening can stay the same while the compass moves.

That is enough.

A stable opening can be used again.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Set a compass opening.
2. Place the compass point at one station.
3. Keep the opening unchanged.
4. Move the compass point to another station.
5. Notice that the pencil point moves with it, but the opening remains the same.

### Diagram 1.1 - Compass Opening Fixed

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Compass with two legs; label compass point, pencil point, fixed opening, unit reach. The diagram should be simple and uncluttered.]

### Diagram 1.2 - Same Opening After Station Transfer

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The same compass opening shown at Station A and then at Station B. Use matching marks to show that the opening has not changed.]

### Core Permission Earned

The same compass opening can be used again after the compass point moves.

### Not Yet

We have not made a boundary yet.

We have not made a triangle yet.

We have not introduced coordinates.

We have not proved that any drawn sides are equal.

We have only kept one opening unchanged while the compass moved.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 001 - FixedRadiusCompassAction; Theorem 001 - Preserved Compass Opening.

---
## Chapter 2 - A Sweep Becomes a Boundary

### Start Here

Choose one point.

Let the compass point stand there.

Now let the pencil move while the opening stays fixed.

The pencil can travel around the standing point. It can move left, right, up, down, and all the way around. But it is never allowed to move closer to the point or farther away from it.

The opening stays the same.

As the pencil moves, it leaves a trace.

That trace is the first boundary.

We do not need a big name yet. We do not need circle theorems. We only need to see what happened.

A point stayed still.

A pencil moved around it.

The reach stayed unchanged.

A boundary appeared.

### What to Notice

Every place on the boundary has the same source.

It was reached from the center by the fixed opening.

That is why the boundary is not just a decoration. It is a record of the opening being used again and again while the center stayed still.

### Chapter Close

The sweep gives the book its first visible object.

A center stays.

A pencil moves.

The opening stays the same.

The boundary is the trace of that unchanged reach.

Now the construction has somewhere to return to.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Choose a point to be the center.
2. Put the compass point on that center.
3. Keep the compass opening fixed.
4. Sweep the pencil around the center.
5. Read the trace as the boundary made by that fixed reach.

### Diagram 2.1 - Center Station and Fixed Opening

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Center O with compass point standing at O. Pencil point is one fixed opening away from O. Label the opening as the same reach that was fixed in Chapter 1.]

### Diagram 2.2 - Sweep Trace

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Pencil sweeps around O and leaves a smooth boundary. Show the compass point still at O.]

### Diagram 2.3 - Boundary as Reached Places

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Several points on the boundary, each joined back to O by the same reach. Use matching marks to show the reach is the same each time.]

### Core Permission Earned

A fixed opening swept from a center makes a boundary.

### Not Yet

We are not using circle theorems yet.

We are not using equations.

We are not saying every possible fact about circles.

We are only saying this: the sweep has made a boundary around the center.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 002 - BaseCircleBoundary; Theorem 002 - Base Boundary Generation.

---
## Chapter 3 - Boundary Positions Become Stations

### Start Here

Look again at the boundary.

It was made by the pencil moving around the center with the opening fixed.

Now choose one point on that boundary.

Call it A.

At first, A is only a chosen place on the trace. But now the compass can stand there.

That matters.

The center was the first station. Now A can be another station.

The opening has not changed. The compass has simply moved to a new place where construction can continue.

So the boundary has become useful in a new way.

It is not only something drawn.

It can give us stations.

### What to Notice

A station is a place where the compass point is allowed to stand.

The point A does not become a station because every possible point is automatically available. It becomes a station because we choose it from the boundary and use it.

The choice is small, but it opens the next action.

We choose a point on the boundary.

Now the compass can stand there.

### Chapter Close

The boundary is no longer only a trace.

Once we choose a point on it, the compass can stand there.

That chosen point becomes a station.

The construction now has a second place to work from.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the boundary from Chapter 2.
2. Choose one point on the boundary.
3. Name the chosen point A.
4. Move the compass point to A.
5. Keep the same opening.
6. Notice that A can now be used as a station.

### Diagram 3.1 - Selecting a Boundary Position

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the boundary around center O. Mark one point on the boundary and label it A. Keep the diagram quiet: one center, one boundary, one chosen point.]

### Diagram 3.2 - Boundary Position Becomes Station

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the compass point moved from O to A. Use the same opening mark as before so the reader sees that the opening has travelled unchanged.]

### Core Permission Earned

A selected point on the boundary can become a new compass station.

### Not Yet

We are not saying that every point on the boundary is already in use.

We are not saying that all possible points have been chosen.

We are not using general circle theory.

We are only saying this:

> When we choose a boundary point, the compass can stand there.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 003 - BoundaryStations; Theorem 003 - Boundary Station Availability.

---
# Part II - The Sixfold Construction

The first part gave us three things.

A fixed opening.

A boundary swept by that opening.

A boundary point that can become a station.

Now the construction can take its first step.

The compass stands at a boundary station. The opening does not change. The pencil sweeps again. Where that sweep meets the first boundary, a new boundary station appears.

One step is not yet a hexagon.

One triangle is not yet a field.

So this part moves slowly. It lets the reader see one step, then one equal-opening triangle, then the repeated steps around the boundary.

The surprise is visible before it is named:

The sixth step returns to the start.

From that return, six triangular cells meet around the center. The outside edge of those cells is the first hexagon.

The construction now carries memory by repetition.

---
## Chapter 4 - The First Boundary Step

### Start Here

We have a center, O.

We have a boundary around O.

We have chosen a point on that boundary and called it A.

Now let the compass stand at A.

Do not change the opening.

The compass is in a new place, but it is carrying the same reach.

Now sweep from A.

The pencil moves around A. As it moves, it can meet the first boundary again.

Choose one meeting point and call it B.

Now look carefully.

A is on the first boundary.

B is on the first boundary.

And A reaches B by the same opening.

That is the first boundary step.

### What to Notice

This chapter earns only one step.

That matters.

We are not rushing to a hexagon.

We are not claiming closure yet.

We are watching one boundary station reach the next boundary station by the same opening.

A reaches B.

That is enough for now.

### Chapter Close

This chapter earns only one step.

That matters.

We are not racing to the hexagon.

We are watching the first boundary station reach the next boundary station by the same opening.

One honest step is enough to begin.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with center O and the boundary around O.
2. Choose a boundary station A.
3. Place the compass point at A.
4. Keep the same opening.
5. Sweep from A.
6. Mark a place where this new sweep meets the original boundary.
7. Call that meeting point B.
8. Notice that A reaches B by the same opening.

### Diagram 4.1 - Sweep from Boundary Station

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show original center O, original boundary, and boundary station A. Add a new sweep centered at A using the same opening.]

### Diagram 4.2 - Meeting with the Base Boundary

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the sweep from A meeting the original boundary at B. Make the meeting point clear.]

### Diagram 4.3 - Equal-Opening Step A to B

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight segment AB. Use the same opening mark used for OA. The reader should see that AB is one use of the same opening.]

### Core Permission Earned

One boundary station can step to another boundary station by the fixed opening.

### Not Yet

We have not proved that the step closes the boundary.

We have not made a hexagon yet.

We have not proved any general rule about chords.

We have only made one boundary step: A to B.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 004 - EqualChordStepping; Theorem 004 - Equal-Opening Boundary Step; Theorems 014-016 - boundary station sweep and unit evidence.

---
## Chapter 5 - The First Triangle

### Start Here

We now have three stations.

There is the center, O.

There is the first boundary station, A.

There is the next boundary station, B.

Look at the three reaches between them.

O reaches A by the fixed opening. That is why A is on the boundary.

O reaches B by the fixed opening. That is why B is on the same boundary.

A reaches B by the fixed opening. That is the step from the last chapter.

So the same opening appears three times.

O to A.

O to B.

A to B.

The three stations now form one small cell.

It has three sides.

Each side has the same source.

This is the first triangle of the book.

### What to Notice

The triangle is not imported.

It does not arrive because it looks familiar.

It appears because the construction has already made three equal reaches.

Three stations.

Three sides.

One opening.

That is the first equal-opening cell.

### Chapter Close

The first triangle is not imported.

It appears because three reaches have already been made by the same opening.

O reaches A.

O reaches B.

A reaches B.

The picture now has its first cell.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with center O.
2. Use boundary stations A and B.
3. Notice that O reaches A by the fixed opening.
4. Notice that O reaches B by the fixed opening.
5. Notice that A reaches B by the fixed opening.
6. Join the three stations as one three-sided cell.
7. Notice that all three sides use the same opening.

### Diagram 5.1 - Three Stations O, A, B

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show O at the center and A and B as adjacent boundary stations. Do not overlabel. The reader should first see the three points.]

### Diagram 5.2 - Three Equal-Opening Sides

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight OA, OB, and AB with the same tick mark or opening mark. The diagram should make 'three sides, one opening' obvious.]

### Diagram 5.3 - First Equal-Opening Cell

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Lightly shade triangle OAB. Keep the equal-opening marks visible.]

### Core Permission Earned

The center and two adjacent boundary stations form the first equal-opening triangle.

### Not Yet

We are not using angle measure.

We are not saying 60 degrees yet.

We are not using triangle theorems from somewhere else.

We are only saying this:

> The three sides of this first triangle are all witnessed by the same compass opening.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 005 - EquilateralCentralCells; Theorem 005 - Central Equal-Opening Cell; Theorems 018-020 - central cell side witnesses.

---
## Chapter 6 - Six Steps Close

### Start Here

Start at one boundary station.

Put the compass point there.

Keep the same opening.

Make the next station on the boundary.

Now stand at that new station.

Keep the opening again.

Make the next station.

Do not widen the compass.

Do not close it.

Do not change the step.

Just keep using the same reach from station to station.

The boundary begins to receive marks.

One step.

Two steps.

Three steps.

Four steps.

Five steps.

The sixth step returns to the first station.

That return matters.

The path has not wandered away. It has closed.

### What to Notice

The closure is not a rule from outside.

It is something the construction shows.

The same opening steps around the boundary, and after six equal steps the walk comes back to where it began.

That gives the book its first closed cycle.

### Chapter Close

The same step repeats.

It does not wander away from the boundary.

It does not change size.

After six steps, it returns to the beginning.

This is the first closed cycle of the book.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a center and its boundary.
2. Choose a boundary station.
3. Use the fixed opening to step to the next boundary station.
4. Repeat the same step around the boundary.
5. Notice that the sixth step returns to the starting station.

### Diagram 6.1 - Boundary Step Sequence

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Boundary with stations A0, A1, A2, A3, A4, and A5 appearing one step at a time. Show the same compass opening between neighboring stations.]

### Diagram 6.2 - Sixth Step Returns

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The final step from A5 back to A0. Make the return visually clear.]

### Diagram 6.3 - Closed Six-Step Boundary Cycle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Completed six-station boundary cycle. Keep it plain. Do not overload it with later labels.]

### Core Permission Earned

The boundary cycle closes after six equal-opening steps.

### Not Yet

We are not using degree measure.

We are not using rotational symmetry as a starting idea.

We are not using general polygon theory.

We have only seen one thing: this equal-opening boundary step closes in six steps.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 006 - SixfoldClosure; Theorem 006 - Six-Step Boundary Closure; Theorems 017, 023-024 - boundary cycle order and closure.

---
## Chapter 7 - Six Triangles Around a Center

### Start Here

Look at the six boundary stations.

Now look back to the center.

The center reaches every boundary station by the same opening. That is how the boundary was made.

Neighboring boundary stations are also one fixed opening apart. That is how the boundary was stepped.

So take the center and two neighboring boundary stations.

They make one equal-opening triangle.

Now move to the next neighboring pair.

The same thing happens again.

Center.

Station.

Next station.

Another triangle.

Keep going around the boundary.

There are six such triangles around the center.

They meet edge to edge.

Together they fill the turn around O.

### What to Notice

The six triangles are not six separate decorations.

They belong to one construction.

Each one uses the center.

Each one uses two neighboring boundary stations.

Each one is made from the same opening.

The construction now carries memory by repetition.

### Chapter Close

The closed boundary can now be read from the center.

Each neighboring pair of boundary stations makes one cell with the center.

Six cells meet around O.

The turn is no longer empty.

It is filled by the construction.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Start with the six boundary stations.
2. Join the center to each boundary station.
3. Read each neighboring boundary pair with the center as one triangular cell.
4. Notice that six such cells meet around the center.

### Diagram 7.1 - Six Central Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Six triangles around center O. Shade or outline the cells lightly.]

### Diagram 7.2 - Adjacent Cells Share a Radius

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two neighboring central cells sharing one center-to-boundary side.]

### Diagram 7.3 - Complete Turn Field

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Full six-cell turn around O. The center should feel like the meeting place of the six cells.]

### Core Permission Earned

Six equal-opening cells fill the turn around the center.

### Not Yet

We are not using degrees yet.

We are not assuming rotational symmetry as a theorem.

We are not importing angle theory.

We are only reading the six cells that the construction has already made.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 007 - CentralTurnField; Theorem 007 - Central Turn Field; Theorems 021-032 - six central cells and turn completion.

---
## Chapter 8 - The Hexagon Inside the Construction

### Start Here

Look only at the six boundary stations.

Connect each station to the next one.

The outside path is a hexagon.

But the name comes after the construction.

First, it is just the path made by six equal-opening steps around the boundary.

The center is still there.

The six triangles are still there.

The hexagon is not separate from them. It is their outside edge.

Now look at the sides of the hexagon.

They do not all run in different ways.

One side has an opposite side that runs the same way.

Another side has an opposite side that runs the same way.

A third side has an opposite side that runs the same way.

So the six sides fall into three same-way pairs.

Those pairs give the first visible directions of the field.

### What to Notice

Direction appears before coordinates.

No axes are needed.

No slope is needed.

No equation is needed.

Just look at the hexagon.

Three pairs of sides.

Three ways the sides run.

Those are the first direction families.

### Chapter Close

The hexagon is not a new stranger.

It is the outside edge of the six central cells.

Its sides also show something the field will need later.

They come in three same-way pairs.

Direction has appeared before coordinates.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the six boundary stations.
2. Connect neighboring stations to make the six-step outline.
3. Keep the center-to-station radii visible.
4. Read the hexagon as the outside of six equal-opening central cells.
5. Notice the three pairs of opposite sides.
6. Read each pair as one visible direction.

### Diagram 8.1 - Boundary Hexagon

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Six boundary stations connected as a hexagonal outline. Keep the center faint or absent in the first version.]

### Diagram 8.2 - Hexagon with Six Radii

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Same hexagon with center O joined to all six boundary stations. Show that the hexagon is carried by the six central cells.]

### Diagram 8.3 - Hexagon as Six Equal-Opening Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Hexagon decomposed into six central triangular cells.]

### Diagram 8.4 - Three Direction Pairs in the Hexagon

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight opposite side pairs of the hexagon in three visual styles. The point is simple: three pairs, three directions.]

### Core Permission Earned

A hexagon can be read as a six-cell source-earned body, and its opposite side pairs give the first visible direction pairs.

### Not Yet

We are not importing regular polygon theory.

We are not using slope.

We are not using coordinates.

We are not saying all facts about parallel lines yet.

We are only noticing the three direction pairs that the hexagon already shows.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 086-089; Theorems 252-255; Affordance 008 - DirectionFamilies.

---
# Part III - The Field Extends

The sixfold construction has closed.

The hexagon is now visible as the outside of six equal-opening cells.

Look at its sides.

They do not run in six unrelated ways. They fall into three same-way pairs.

That is the quiet beginning of direction.

This part does not begin with axes. It does not begin with slope. It does not begin with a coordinate grid.

It begins with the hexagon.

The three visible directions can continue. When they continue and meet again, more stations appear. More triangular cells appear. The first construction grows outward.

The result is a triangular field.

This field is not a background decoration. It is the place where later geometry, rightness, ratio, coordinates, and algebra will find their first support.

---
## Chapter 9 - Direction Appears

### Start Here

Look at the hexagon.

It has six sides, but the sides do not all run in six unrelated ways.

Two of them run one way.

Two of them run another way.

Two of them run a third way.

So the hexagon gives us three pairs of matching directions.

This is the easiest place to see direction.

We are not starting with a coordinate grid. We are not measuring slope. We are not using equations for lines.

We are only looking.

One side runs across the field. The side opposite it runs the same way.

A second pair runs another way.

A third pair runs another way.

That gives us three directions.

Here, direction means something simple:

This side goes the same way as that side.

### What to Notice

A direction family is not a new object dropped onto the page.

It is a group of lines or segments that the field lets us recognize as going the same way.

The hexagon gives the first clear picture.

Later, those directions will continue beyond the first hexagon.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Start with the six-station boundary cycle.
2. Connect adjacent boundary stations to form the hexagon.
3. Compare opposite sides.
4. Notice that the six sides fall into three pairs.
5. Treat each pair as the first visible sign of a direction family.

### Diagram 9.1 - Direction Pairs in the Hexagon

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A hexagon with opposite sides highlighted in matching styles. Show three pairs: one pair horizontal or nearly horizontal, and two slanted pairs. Keep the center and radii faint or optional.]

### Diagram 9.2 - Three Direction Families

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The same hexagon with the three direction families extended lightly through the field. Use three line styles, not heavy color dependence.]

### Diagram 9.3 - Direction Family Without Coordinates

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Same-direction recurrence shown without axes, numbers, or slope. The reader should see that direction is recognized by repeated way-of-running.]

### Chapter Close

The hexagon has done more than close a path.

It has shown direction.

Not with numbers.

Not with axes.

Just with sides that run the same way.

Those same-way sides give the field its first direction families.

### Core Permission Earned

Recurring same-way lines may be gathered into direction families.

### Not Yet

We are not using slope yet.

We are not using coordinate axes.

We are not importing a full theory of parallel lines.

We have only noticed same-way recurrence inside the constructed hexagon and field.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 008 - DirectionFamilies; Theorems 033-034 - repeated direction and three direction families.

---
## Chapter 10 - Extending the Field

### Start Here

The hexagon gave us three directions.

Now let one direction keep going.

Then let another direction keep going.

Where they meet, a new station appears.

This is not a random point. It is a meeting made by directions the construction has already earned.

Now let the third direction join the picture.

The field begins to repeat.

A triangle appears beside the first triangle.

Then another.

Then another.

The first construction has not been replaced.

It has been extended.

### What to Notice

The page is not being filled by a square grid.

It is not being filled by arbitrary decoration.

The field grows because the three earned directions continue and meet.

Where directions meet, stations appear.

Where stations repeat, triangular cells appear.

The first triangle taught us a cell.

The hexagon showed us the directions.

Now the directions carry the cell outward.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the hexagon and its three direction families.
2. Extend one family beyond the first boundary.
3. Extend a second family until it meets the first.
4. Mark the new meeting as a station.
5. Continue the third family through the same structure.
6. Notice repeated triangular cells appearing outside the first hexagon.

### Diagram 10.1 - Extending One Direction Family

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One pair of same-direction lines from the hexagon extended beyond the boundary. Keep everything else light.]

### Diagram 10.2 - Two Families Meet

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two extended direction families meeting outside the first hexagon, with new stations marked at their crossings.]

### Diagram 10.3 - Triangular Tessellation Emerges

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Repeated triangular cells appearing beyond the first hexagon. Show that they share the same three directions.]

### Diagram 10.4 - First Field vs Extended Field

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Original sixfold construction emphasized inside a larger triangular field. The reader should see that the large field grows from the first construction.]

### Chapter Close

The first hexagon is not the end.

Its directions can continue.

Where continued directions meet, new stations appear.

Where stations repeat, new cells appear.

The field grows because the construction has a way to keep going.

### Core Permission Earned

The sixfold construction can extend into a triangular field.

### Not Yet

We are not using arbitrary tilings.

We are not using a coordinate grid as the source.

We are not saying that any repeated pattern is allowed.

Only the field grown from the earned directions is allowed here.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 011 - TriangularTessellation; Theorems 035-037 and 053-057 - field extension and triangular-cell recurrence.

---
## Chapter 11 - Transport Without Cheating

### Start Here

The field repeats.

That is useful.

But it can also tempt us to cheat.

We might see one triangle and say:

That other triangle looks the same, so it must have the same facts.

This book will not do that.

Looking the same is not enough.

A relation can travel only when the reason for the relation travels with it.

If one cell has a side made by the fixed opening, then another cell may use the same claim only when that same support is present.

If one triangle is carried by the same direction-family structure as another, then some relations may travel.

But if a shape merely looks similar, and we do not know how it was made, we do not copy the fact.

### What to Notice

A claim needs a receipt.

The receipt is the constructional reason the claim is true.

When a claim moves to another part of the field, its receipt must move too.

That is transport without cheating.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Mark a relation inside one cell.
2. Identify the constructional reason for that relation.
3. Find another cell with the same supporting structure.
4. Transport the relation only if the support is present.
5. Reject copies based on appearance alone.

### Diagram 11.1 - Local Relation in One Cell

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One triangular cell with a side or relation marked. Include a small note showing why the relation is earned.]

### Diagram 11.2 - Lawful Transport to Another Cell

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A second cell in the same field with the same source condition highlighted. Show the relation traveling by structure, not by appearance.]

### Diagram 11.3 - Invalid Copy by Appearance

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A visually similar shape outside or unsupported by the field. Mark it gently as not licensed.]

### Chapter Close

The field lets relations travel.

But not by wishful thinking.

A claim can move only when its support moves too.

That rule will protect everything that comes later.

The receipt must travel with the claim.

### Core Permission Earned

Local relations can travel across the field only when their source conditions travel with them.

### Not Yet

We are not copying by resemblance.

We are not assuming symmetry because a picture looks neat.

We are not treating the diagram as proof unless the constructional support is present.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 012 - Transportability; Theorems 038-042 - transported relation and non-arbitrary transport.

---
# Part IV - Geometry from the Field

We are no longer making only the first field.

Now we begin to read what the field has already made.

A triangle can be read as one earned cell.

Two neighboring cells can be read together as a rhombus.

The six central cells can be read together as a hexagon.

This is the beginning of geometry in the book.

The figure does not create its own authority. The name does not create the property. The field comes first.

A figure is trusted when its sides, cells, and relations can be seen in the construction.

This part teaches the reader to look at sides, shared sides, adjacent cells, paths, and area by cell count.

The formulas can wait.

The cells are already there.

---
## Chapter 12 - Figures as Source-Earned Bodies

### Start Here

Look again at the field.

Do not add anything new yet.

The first triangle is already there.

The hexagon is already there.

Shared sides are already there.

Repeated directions are already there.

So geometry does not arrive as a stranger.

Geometry begins when we look at what the construction has already made and say:

This shape can now be read as a figure.

A triangle is not trusted because it looks like a triangle.

It is trusted because its sides have receipts in the construction.

A rhombus is not trusted because it looks neat.

It is trusted when we can see it as two earned triangular cells sharing a side.

A hexagon is not imported from a polygon chapter.

It is already sitting inside the first construction.

### What to Notice

The field comes first.

The figure comes after.

The construction makes the body.

Geometry names and studies the body.

That order protects the book from using names too early.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the triangular field already constructed.
2. Select one earned triangular cell.
3. Select two adjacent cells and read them together as a rhombus.
4. Select the six central cells and read them together as a hexagon.
5. Keep the construction visible behind each figure.

### Diagram 12.1 - Triangle as Earned Cell

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One triangular cell from the field. Keep the field faintly visible behind it. Mark the three source-earned sides.]

### Diagram 12.2 - Rhombus as Two Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two adjacent triangular cells sharing one side. Shade the two cells together as one rhombus.]

### Diagram 12.3 - Hexagon as Six Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The central hexagon shown as six triangular cells around the center.]

### Chapter Close

Geometry begins here without arriving from outside.

The field already has cells.

It already has shared sides.

It already has larger bodies.

Now we are allowed to read those bodies as figures.

The construction makes the figure.

Geometry names what is there.

### Core Permission Earned

Geometry may read constructed figures as source-earned bodies.

### Not Yet

We are not trusting a figure because it looks right.

We are not importing every theorem about triangles, rhombi, or hexagons.

We are not letting a name create a property.

We are only saying this: when the field has already built the parts, geometry may read the figure.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Bridge Blocks 256-271 - post-255 figure-reading bridge.

---
## Chapter 13 - Sides, Adjacency, and Paths

### Start Here

Look at two triangular cells that touch.

They are not only near each other.

They share a whole side.

That shared side matters.

A side can belong to one cell.

The same side can also sit between two cells.

When two cells share a side, they are neighbors.

Now the field has a way to move.

You can step from one cell to the next through a shared side.

Then you can step again.

A path is not a road drawn on top of the field.

A path is a lawful walk through cells that the field has already joined.

### What to Notice

A side can do two jobs.

On the outside of a figure, it acts like an edge.

Between two cells, it acts like a passage.

That is how adjacency begins.

Two cells are adjacent when they share a side.

A path is a chain of such side-to-side passages.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Select two neighboring triangular cells.
2. Mark the side they share.
3. Read the two cells as adjacent.
4. Select a chain of cells.
5. Follow a path through shared sides.
6. Distinguish an outside boundary side from an inside passage side.

### Diagram 13.1 - Shared Side Between Adjacent Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two triangular cells sharing one side. Mark the shared side clearly.]

### Diagram 13.2 - Path Across Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A chain of triangular cells. Show a path passing from cell to cell through shared sides.]

### Diagram 13.3 - Boundary Path vs Interior Path

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show one path along the outside boundary and another path through interior shared sides.]

### Chapter Close

A field is not only a set of cells.

It also has ways to move.

A side can be an edge.

A shared side can be a passage.

A path is a lawful walk through what the field has already joined.

### Core Permission Earned

The triangular field supports sides, shared sides, adjacency, and paths.

### Not Yet

We are not saying that all paths are the same.

We are not saying which path is shortest.

We are not importing graph theory or coordinate distance.

We are only learning how the field lets one cell meet another.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 047-052 - side, adjacency, and path support.

---
## Chapter 14 - Area Begins with Cells

### Start Here

Look at one triangular cell.

For now, treat it as one small piece of area.

Do not ask for a formula yet.

Do not ask for base times height.

Do not ask for square units.

Just look at the cell.

It is a repeatable piece.

The same kind of piece appears again and again in the field.

Now put two such cells together.

If they share a side, they can make a rhombus.

So the rhombus has two cells of area.

Now look at the central hexagon.

It is made of six triangular cells around the center.

So the hexagon has six cells of area.

### What to Notice

Area can begin by counting structure.

One cell.

Two cells.

Six cells.

More cells.

A formula may come later. But the first meaning is visible before the formula appears.

The reader can point to the pieces and count them.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Select one triangular cell as the native area seed.
2. Count one cell as one native area piece.
3. Read a rhombus as two cells.
4. Read the central hexagon as six cells.
5. Read larger regions by lawful decomposition into cells.

### Diagram 14.1 - One Cell as Area Seed

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One triangular cell shaded as the first area piece.]

### Diagram 14.2 - Rhombus Equals Two Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A rhombus split into two triangular cells. Label it as two cells.]

### Diagram 14.3 - Hexagon Equals Six Cells

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The central hexagon split into six triangular cells. Label it as six cells.]

### Diagram 14.4 - Larger Region by Cell Count

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A larger region in the triangular field, shaded and counted by cells.]

### Chapter Close

Area does not need to begin with a formula.

It can begin with one cell.

Then two.

Then six.

Then any region the field lets us build and count honestly.

The formula can come later.

The counted structure comes first.

### Core Permission Earned

Area may be read by lawful cell count before formula.

### Not Yet

We are not using area formulas yet.

We are not measuring arbitrary curved regions.

We are not claiming that every shape has already been measured.

We are only saying this: when a region is made from earned cells, we can begin by counting the cells.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 090 and 252-255 - cell-count area and recomposition support.

---
# Part V - Rightness and Ratio

The field has stations, cells, paths, and directions.

Now we look for clean meetings inside it.

Some selected connectors meet in a special way. One connector does not slide along the other. It cuts across it cleanly.

The picture comes before the word.

Later, we may call this perpendicular. Here, the reader first sees a right meeting.

A right meeting lets us read a right triangle from the field. That triangle has sides. The sides can be compared.

Those comparisons are ratios.

This part prepares trigonometry without starting from trigonometry.

The ratio comes before the trigonometric name.

---
## Chapter 15 - Right Meetings in the Field

### Start Here

Look back at the field.

It has stations.

It has cells.

It has directions.

It has paths that can be followed.

Now draw one connector through the field.

Draw another connector that meets it.

At first, they are just two lines meeting.

But some meetings are special.

One line does not slide along the other.

It cuts across it cleanly.

The meeting feels square.

We do not need the word perpendicular yet.

We do not need slope.

We do not need equations.

We only need the picture.

One connector goes this way.

Another connector meets it without leaning with it.

That is a right meeting.

### What to Notice

The right mark comes after the meeting.

It is not decoration.

It is a receipt.

It says:

The field supports this meeting.

That order matters.

First the stations.

Then the connectors.

Then the meeting.

Then the mark.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the sixfold field.
2. Select connector lines between stations already supplied by the construction.
3. Let selected connectors meet.
4. Look for the clean crossing supported by the field.
5. Mark the meeting as right only after the support is visible.

### Diagram 15.1 - Opposite Boundary Connectors

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the sixfold field with selected connectors between opposite boundary stations. Keep the diagram uncluttered. The reader should see the connectors before seeing any right-angle mark.]

### Diagram 15.2 - Selected Connectors Meeting at Center

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show two selected connectors meeting at the center or inside the field. Highlight the meeting point.]

### Diagram 15.3 - Right-Meeting Evidence

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Add the right-angle marker only after the selected connectors are visible. The marker should feel like a label on earned structure, not an assumption.]

### Chapter Close

Rightness is not a sticker we place on a corner because it looks square.

The field gives stations.

The stations give connectors.

Some connectors meet cleanly.

Only then do we mark the meeting as right.

The mark remembers what the field has shown.

### Core Permission Earned

Selected connectors inside the constructed field may meet right.

### Not Yet

We are not using slope.

We are not using coordinate perpendicularity.

We are not using general right-angle theory.

We are not calling every square-looking corner right.

We are only marking rightness where the constructed field supports it.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 010 - PerpendicularConnectors; Theorem 010 - Perpendicular Connector Evidence.

---
## Chapter 16 - The First Field-Right Triangle

### Start Here

We have a right meeting.

Now use it.

Choose one point along one connector.

Choose another point along the other connector.

Join those points.

A triangle appears around the right meeting.

The right meeting is one corner of the triangle.

The two chosen stations make the other corners.

One side lies along the first connector.

Another side lies along the second connector.

The last side reaches across from one chosen station to the other.

The crossing corner is right.

So this is a right triangle.

But it is not just any right triangle.

It came from the field.

### What to Notice

The triangle is not floating by itself.

The field gave the stations.

The field gave the connectors.

The field gave the right meeting.

So the triangle carries its source with it.

Later, we will name side roles.

One side will be the hypotenuse.

Other sides will be opposite or adjacent, depending on which angle we choose.

But first, see the triangle sitting inside the field.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a right meeting inside the constructed field.
2. Choose one station on one connector.
3. Choose another station on the other connector.
4. Join the chosen stations.
5. Read the triangle as a field-right triangle.
6. Keep the field visible behind the triangle.

### Diagram 16.1 - Extracting a Right Triangle from the Field

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the full field faintly, then highlight one right triangle inside it. The right meeting should be visible as one corner.]

### Diagram 16.2 - Side Roles

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the same triangle with the side across from the right corner identified as the longest side. Do not overload with trigonometric labels yet.]

### Diagram 16.3 - Field Source Behind the Triangle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the triangle again with the field still visible behind it, so the reader sees that the triangle was extracted from the field.]

### Chapter Close

The right meeting now gives us a triangle.

But the triangle is not floating by itself.

It still belongs to the field.

Its sides come from selected connectors.

Its right corner comes from an earned meeting.

That is why it can carry ratio later.

### Core Permission Earned

A field-right triangle can be read from a right meeting inside the constructed field.

### Not Yet

We are not starting trigonometry yet.

We are not using sine, cosine, or tangent.

We are not using the unit circle.

We are not treating right triangles as detached objects.

We are only extracting one right triangle from the field.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 395-398 - first field-right triangle side roles and first native ratio packet; Theorem 417 - native trigonometry begins with field-right ratio.

## Chapter 17 - The First Ratio Packet

### Start Here

The field has given us a right triangle.

Now look at its sides.

One side is short.

One side is longer.

One side reaches across from the right meeting.

In the first important case, the side packet is:

> 1, sqrt(3), 2

Do not rush to the names.

First, compare the sides.

Choose one sharp corner.

From that corner, one side is beside you.

One side is across from you.

The longest side is across from the right meeting.

Now choose the other sharp corner.

The nearby side and across side switch roles.

The triangle did not change.

Only your chosen corner changed.

### What to Notice

Ratio begins as comparison.

Across side compared with long side.

Nearby side compared with long side.

Across side compared with nearby side.

These comparisons are already present before sine, cosine, or tangent are named.

The ratios come first.

The names come later.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a field-right triangle.
2. Identify the side across from the right meeting.
3. Choose one acute corner.
4. Read one side as across from that corner.
5. Read one side as beside that corner.
6. Compare sides by ratio before naming the comparisons.

### Diagram 17.1 - 1, sqrt(3), 2 Triangle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show one field-right triangle labeled with side packet 1, sqrt(3), and 2. Keep it embedded in or visibly linked to the field.]

### Diagram 17.2 - Same Triangle, Different Chosen Angle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the same triangle twice, choosing a different acute angle each time. Make clear that opposite and adjacent roles switch.]

### Diagram 17.3 - Ratio Packet Before Names

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show side comparisons as plain ratios before using sine, cosine, or tangent.]

### Chapter Close

The triangle now has sides we can compare.

Do not rush to the names.

First, see the packet.

One side.

Another side.

The long side across from the right meeting.

The ratios are already there.

The names can wait one more chapter.

### Core Permission Earned

Ratio is available as an angle-bound comparison inside a field-right triangle.

### Not Yet

We are not using trigonometric function names yet.

We are not starting from the unit circle.

We are not treating ratio as memorized table data.

We are only comparing sides that the field-right triangle has already supplied.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 395-399 - hypotenuse role, acute-side roles, side comparison, first native ratio packet, ratio before names; Theorem 417.

# Part VI - Trigonometry

The right triangle is already visible.

The side comparisons are already visible.

Now the names can arrive.

Sine, cosine, and tangent should not feel like magic words. They are names for comparisons inside a right triangle.

The triangle comes first.

The ratios come next.

The names come after.

This part keeps that order.

It also lets the unit circle appear later as an organizer. The unit circle is useful, but it is not the first source of trigonometry in this book.

The source is still the constructed field and the right triangle read from it.

---
## Chapter 18 - Sine, Cosine, Tangent

### Start Here

We already have the triangle.

We already have the side roles.

We already have the ratios.

Now we give three ratios their common names.

Choose one sharp angle.

The side across from that angle is the opposite side.

The side beside that angle is the adjacent side.

The side across from the right meeting is the hypotenuse.

Now name the comparisons.

Opposite compared with hypotenuse is called sine.

Adjacent compared with hypotenuse is called cosine.

Opposite compared with adjacent is called tangent.

That is all the names are doing at first.

They are labels for comparisons the triangle already had.

### What to Notice

Sine, cosine, and tangent are not new sources.

They do not create the ratios.

They name them.

When you see sine, remember a side comparison.

When you see cosine, remember a side comparison.

When you see tangent, remember a side comparison.

The picture carries the memory.

The names help us speak about it.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a field-right triangle.
2. Choose one acute angle.
3. Name the side across from that angle as opposite.
4. Name the side beside that angle as adjacent.
5. Name the side across from the right meeting as hypotenuse.
6. Name the three already-visible ratios as sine, cosine, and tangent.

### Diagram 18.1 - Sine as Opposite over Hypotenuse

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight the opposite side and hypotenuse relative to one chosen acute angle.]

### Diagram 18.2 - Cosine as Adjacent over Hypotenuse

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight the adjacent side and hypotenuse relative to the same chosen angle.]

### Diagram 18.3 - Tangent as Opposite over Adjacent

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Highlight the opposite and adjacent sides relative to the same chosen angle.]

### Diagram 18.4 - Three Ratio Names on One Triangle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One summary triangle with the three named comparisons shown cleanly. Avoid clutter by using callouts if needed.]

### Chapter Close

Sine, cosine, and tangent are not new sources.

They are names.

Sine names one side comparison.

Cosine names another.

Tangent names another.

The triangle already had the comparisons.

The names help us speak about them.

### Core Permission Earned

Sine, cosine, and tangent may be used as names for field-earned side ratios.

### Not Yet

We are not treating sine, cosine, and tangent as primitives.

We are not beginning with the unit circle.

We are not asking the reader to memorize a table without seeing the triangle.

The names record ratios that have already appeared.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 409-418 - trigonometric naming records earned ratios; sine, cosine, tangent, and local naming permission.

---
## Chapter 19 - Trigonometric Relations

### Start Here

The three trigonometric names come from one triangle.

That is why they know each other.

Sine uses the opposite side and the hypotenuse.

Cosine uses the adjacent side and the hypotenuse.

Tangent uses the opposite side and the adjacent side.

Notice the repetition.

The opposite side appears in sine and tangent.

The adjacent side appears in cosine and tangent.

The hypotenuse appears in sine and cosine.

So the ratios are not strangers.

They are different readings of the same side packet.

### What to Notice

A trigonometric relation is not just symbol play.

It is memory of one triangle.

The same sides keep appearing in different comparisons.

Later, algebra will transform these records.

But the symbols should not float away from the picture.

The picture comes first.

The relation remembers it.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with one field-right triangle and one chosen acute angle.
2. Read sine as opposite compared with hypotenuse.
3. Read cosine as adjacent compared with hypotenuse.
4. Read tangent as opposite compared with adjacent.
5. Notice which sides are reused.
6. Treat relations among the names as memory of the same triangle.

### Diagram 19.1 - Ratio Family from One Triangle

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show sine, cosine, and tangent as three callouts from the same triangle. Make the shared sides visually obvious.]

### Diagram 19.2 - Complementary Side Role Exchange

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show the two acute angles in the same right triangle. Show how opposite and adjacent roles switch when the chosen angle changes.]

### Diagram 19.3 - Tangent as Ratio of Ratio Records

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Show tangent connected to sine and cosine through shared side comparisons. Keep the diagram visual before symbolic.]

### Chapter Close

The three trigonometric names remember one triangle.

That is why they are related.

They reuse the same sides in different comparisons.

A trigonometric relation is not just symbol play.

It is compressed memory of the picture.

### Core Permission Earned

Basic trigonometric relations may be treated as compressed memory of one field-earned right triangle.

### Not Yet

We are not doing detached identity manipulation yet.

We are not treating formulas as the source.

We are not replacing the triangle with symbols too quickly.

The relation must still remember the picture.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 409-418 - named ratios as records of one field-right triangle; Theorems 419 onward for later trigonometric material.

---
## Chapter 20 - The Unit Circle Comes Later

### Start Here

Look back at the right triangle.

It gave us side comparisons.

Those comparisons became sine, cosine, and tangent.

Now draw a circle with radius 1.

Choose one radius.

Let it lean out from the center.

Drop a vertical line from the end of the radius down to the horizontal line through the center.

A right triangle appears inside the circle.

The radius is the hypotenuse.

Because the radius is 1, the side ratios become especially easy to record.

The horizontal reach records the cosine.

The vertical reach records the sine.

The circle did not invent those ratios.

It gives them a clean place to live.

### What to Notice

The unit circle comes later on purpose.

It is useful.

It is clean.

It organizes the ratios well.

But it does not create them.

The field-right triangle gave the ratios first.

The circle helps us remember and organize them.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with the field-right triangle and its side ratios.
2. Draw a circle of radius 1.
3. Choose a radius from the center to the boundary.
4. Drop a perpendicular to form a right triangle.
5. Read horizontal and vertical reaches as ratio records.

### Diagram 20.1 - Unit Circle as Organizer

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Unit circle with center O, radius 1, and a chosen radius leaning from the center to the boundary. Keep the picture clean.]

### Diagram 20.2 - Projection Coordinates

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: From the end of the radius, drop a vertical line to the horizontal axis. Show horizontal reach and vertical reach.]

### Diagram 20.3 - Triangle Ratio to Circle Coordinate

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Same right triangle shown first as a triangle, then placed inside the unit circle.]

### Diagram 20.4 - Unit Circle Overlaid on Source Field

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Unit circle lightly overlaid on the constructed field. The circle organizes; it does not replace the field.]

### Chapter Close

The unit circle comes later on purpose.

It is useful.

It is clean.

It organizes the ratios well.

But it does not invent them.

The field-right triangle gave the ratios first.

The circle gives them a place to live.

### Core Permission Earned

The unit circle may organize already-earned trigonometric ratios.

### Not Yet

We are not treating the unit circle as the first source of trigonometry.

We are not forgetting the right triangle.

We are not replacing the field with coordinates.

We are only using the unit circle as a clear organizing picture.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 414-415 - trigonometric names do not import unit-circle theory; coordinate projection remains later audit.

# Part VII - Coordinate Geometry

The field exists before coordinates.

The stations are already there. The steps are already there. The direction families are already there.

Coordinates arrive as records.

A coordinate can help us find a station again. A coordinate difference can record a move. A norm can audit a distance. A slope can record a visible direction.

But the coordinate label does not create the station.

The equation of a line does not create the direction.

This part keeps the order clear:

The picture builds.

The coordinate record checks.

---
## Chapter 21 - Coordinates as Records

### Start Here

Look at the field before there are coordinates.

There are stations.

There are steps.

There are cells.

There are directions.

Nothing is missing from the construction.

Now add labels.

One station may be called (0, 0).

Another may be called (1, 0).

Another may be called (0, 1), or by whatever coordinate record the field is using.

The labels are useful.

They help us find stations again.

They help us compare one station with another.

They help us check distance and direction.

But the label does not create the station.

The station was already there.

### What to Notice

A coordinate is like a name tag.

A name tag can help you remember who someone is.

But the name tag does not create the person.

In the same way, a coordinate can help us remember where a station is.

But the coordinate does not create the station.

Coordinates come after the picture.

They help us check what is already there.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with constructed stations in the field.
2. Choose a coordinate origin.
3. Assign coordinate labels to selected stations.
4. Use the labels as records.
5. Keep the construction as the source.

### Diagram 21.1 - Constructed Stations Before Coordinates

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A small triangular field with stations and cells but no axes or coordinate labels.]

### Diagram 21.2 - Coordinates Added Afterward

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Same field with coordinate labels added after the construction is visible.]

### Diagram 21.3 - Coordinate Label as Memory Tag

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One station shown with both its constructional role and its coordinate label.]

### Chapter Close

Coordinates arrive after the field.

That order matters.

The stations are already there.

The cells are already there.

The directions are already there.

A coordinate label helps us remember and check a position.

It does not create the position.

### Core Permission Earned

Coordinate labels may record and check constructed positions.

### Not Yet

Coordinates do not generate the field.

Coordinates do not create stations.

Coordinates do not replace constructional evidence.

They are records and checks.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 009 - CoordinateAudit; Theorem 009 - Coordinate Audit; Theorem 480 - coordinate audit support.

---
## Chapter 22 - Differences and Distance Checks

### Start Here

Choose two stations in the field.

Each station has a coordinate label.

The first label tells where the move starts.

The second label tells where the move ends.

To compare them, look at what changed.

How far did the record move in one direction?

How far did it move in the other direction?

Those changes make a difference record.

The difference record is not a new piece of geometry.

It is a way to remember the move from one station to another.

Now we can check distance.

A distance check asks whether the coordinate record agrees with the construction.

If the construction says two stations are one fixed opening apart, the coordinate check should agree.

If it does not agree, something has gone wrong.

### What to Notice

Coordinates do not take authority away from the construction.

They audit it.

The picture builds.

The coordinate record checks.

That is why coordinates are powerful without becoming the source.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Choose two constructed stations.
2. Read the coordinate label of each station.
3. Subtract the start record from the end record.
4. Treat the result as a difference record.
5. Use the field norm or distance rule to audit the constructed distance.

### Diagram 22.1 - Two Coordinate Records

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two stations in the field, each with a coordinate label.]

### Diagram 22.2 - Difference Vector

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Arrow from the first station to the second station, labelled as the difference between the two coordinate records.]

### Diagram 22.3 - Norm as Distance Audit

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Same arrow checked against a distance or norm rule. Keep the construction visible underneath.]

### Diagram 22.4 - Projection Components

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Difference broken into field-direction components.]

### Chapter Close

A coordinate difference records a move.

It tells how the record changes from one station to another.

A norm checks the length of that move.

But the check is not the source.

The construction is still the source.

The coordinate record audits it.

### Core Permission Earned

Coordinate differences can record movement, and distance rules can audit constructed distance.

### Not Yet

We are not letting coordinate calculation replace the construction.

We are not treating every algebraic number as an automatically constructed length.

We are only using coordinates to check positions and movements already tied to the field.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 154-159 - coordinate ledger, native squared norm, native norm, and unit-distance audit; Theorem 480.

## Chapter 23 - Lines, Slopes, and Direction Families

### Start Here

Look back at the hexagon.

Its sides came in three pairs.

Each pair went the same way.

That gave us the first direction families.

Then the field extended.

The same directions continued across more stations.

Lines of stations appeared again and again.

Now coordinates can record that recurrence.

If we move from one station to another along the same kind of line, the coordinate difference has a pattern.

That pattern can be recorded.

In ordinary coordinate geometry, one way to record direction is called slope.

But slope does not come first here.

The line in the field comes first.

The repeated direction comes first.

The slope is a later record of what the field already shows.

### What to Notice

Slope is not magic.

It is a memory tag for a visible direction.

A line equation is not a creator of the line.

It is a record of stations and direction that the field has already made available.

Direction came before slope.

The field came before the equation.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a visible direction family in the field.
2. Choose two stations on that direction family.
3. Record their coordinate difference.
4. Notice the repeated difference pattern along the same family.
5. Name the coordinate direction record as slope when using rectangular coordinates.

### Diagram 23.1 - Direction Family Before Slope

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One direction family highlighted in the triangular field, without axes.]

### Diagram 23.2 - Slope as Coordinate Record

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Axes or coordinate labels added after the direction family is visible. Show slope as a record, not a source.]

### Diagram 23.3 - Line Equation as Direction Memory

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Several stations on the same line with a simple equation label beside them.]

### Chapter Close

Direction came before slope.

The field already showed lines that run the same way.

Coordinates can record that direction.

A slope or line equation is useful because it remembers a visible relation.

It does not create the relation.

### Core Permission Earned

Analytic line language may record native direction-family structure.

### Not Yet

Slope is not the first source of direction.

Line equations do not create the field.

Coordinates do not replace the visible direction families.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Affordance 008; Theorems 033-037 - direction families and extension; Theorems 154-159 - coordinate ledger and native norm; Theorem 480.
