# Part VIII - Algebra

Algebra begins as memory.

A symbol remembers a relation that has already been seen, built, measured, or constrained.

An equation holds a relation still when one part is unknown.

A transformation changes the form of a relation without changing its truth.

That is why algebra belongs at the end of the main path.

The book began with one fixed opening.

Something could move, but the opening stayed the same.

Now symbols can move.

Forms can change.

But the relation must stay true.

That is the final permission of the main book.

---
## Chapter 24 - Symbols Remember Relations

### Start Here

Look at a constructed relation.

Maybe two sides are the same opening.

Maybe a triangle has a side packet.

Maybe a coordinate difference records a movement.

At first, the relation lives in the picture.

Now give part of it a symbol.

Call a side a.

Call a missing length x.

Call a fixed reach r.

The symbol is small, but it carries memory.

It points back to something.

That is the first rule of algebra in this book:

Algebra remembers relations.

It does not invent them.

### What to Notice

A good symbol is like a folded diagram.

It does not show every line anymore.

But it should still remember the relation it came from.

That is why algebra can be powerful without becoming detached.

The picture may become lighter.

The relation stays.

The symbol carries it forward.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a visible relation in a figure or field.
2. Choose one part of the relation to name with a symbol.
3. Keep the source relation attached to the symbol.
4. Use the symbol as a memory token.
5. Do not let the symbol float away from its meaning.

### Diagram 24.1 - From Constructed Relation to Symbol

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A constructed side relation shown in a figure, then labeled with a symbol.]

### Diagram 24.2 - Symbol as Memory Token

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The symbol shown beside the figure with an arrow back to the relation it remembers.]

### Diagram 24.3 - Formula as Compressed Diagram

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A figure on one side and a short symbolic relation on the other. The formula should feel like a compressed record of the figure.]

### Chapter Close

A symbol is small, but it should not be empty.

It points back.

It remembers a side, a distance, a ratio, a movement, or another relation the construction has shown.

That is how algebra begins here.

Not as free marks.

As memory.

### Core Permission Earned

Symbols may carry source-earned relation memory.

### Not Yet

Symbols do not create relations.

Algebra is not yet free symbol-shuffling.

A formula should not be treated as authority unless its relation is understood.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 154-155 - coordinate ledger as durable record and algebraic memory; Theorems 584-587 - equation and lawful transformation support.

---
## Chapter 25 - Equations and Unknowns

### Start Here

Look at a figure from the field.

Some parts are already known.

Maybe one side is known.

Maybe one distance is known.

Maybe one ratio is known.

One part is missing.

We do not panic.

We do not guess.

We give the missing part a name.

Call it x.

The letter x does not create a new relation.

It marks the part we do not know yet.

Now the figure can speak in symbols.

It can say:

This known part and this unknown part must fit together.

That sentence becomes an equation.

### What to Notice

An equation is a way to hold a relation still.

It says that two records must name the same amount, or that several parts must fit one structure.

The equation is not a trick.

It is a memory of the picture.

Solving means finding the value that lets the relation stay true.

The answer belongs back in the picture.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a constructed relation.
2. Mark one missing part with a symbol.
3. Write an equation that records the relation.
4. Transform the equation only in ways that preserve equality.
5. Read the recovered value back into the figure.

### Diagram 25.1 - Missing Relation Marked as Unknown

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: A known structure with one side, distance, coordinate, or ratio marked x. The diagram should show that x belongs to an existing relation. It should not float by itself.]

### Diagram 25.2 - Equation as Constraint

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The same structure beside an equation. Use arrows or matching labels to show which parts of the picture became symbols.]

### Diagram 25.3 - Solving Recovers the Missing Relation

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: The original structure after x has been found. Show the value returned to the place where the unknown stood.]

### Core Permission Earned

Equations can constrain and recover unknown relations.

### Not Yet

We are not solving by guessing.

We are not moving symbols just because a rule says so.

We are not letting algebra detach from the construction.

Every algebraic move must preserve the relation it is carrying.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 584-585 - equations, unknowns, and relation recovery.

---
## Chapter 26 - Lawful Transformation

### Start Here

Look at an equation.

It is a record of a relation.

The relation may have started as a length in a figure, a distance in the field, a ratio in a triangle, a coordinate check, or a balance between two expressions.

Now it sits on the page in symbols.

That is useful.

Symbols can be copied, rearranged, compared, and reused.

But symbols are dangerous if they forget what they are carrying.

So the rule is firm:

A transformation is allowed only when it keeps the relation true.

That is what lawful means here.

The form may change.

The truth may not.

### What to Notice

Suppose two expressions are equal.

If we add the same amount to both, they remain equal.

If we subtract the same amount from both, they remain equal.

If we multiply both by the same amount, they remain equal.

If we divide both by the same nonzero amount, they remain equal.

Each move changes the look of the equation.

But it does not break the equality.

That is the heart of algebra here.

Algebra carries an earned relation through different forms.

It does not make truth from nowhere.

### Mathematical Construction

1. Begin with a source-earned relation.
2. Record it symbolically.
3. Apply one transformation at a time.
4. Check that each transformation preserves equality or relation.
5. Use the new form to see something the old form hid.

### Diagram 26.1 - Same Relation, Different Forms

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: One relation shown in several equivalent symbolic forms. Each form should be connected back to the same source relation.]

### Diagram 26.2 - Lawful vs Unlawful Transformation

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Two-column diagram. Left column: a transformation that preserves equality. Right column: a move that changes only one side and breaks equality.]

### Diagram 26.3 - Final Branch Convergence

[DIAGRAM PLACEHOLDER: Four branches returning to one source-bound view. Geometry = constructed figure. Trigonometry = stable ratio. Coordinate geometry = audited position. Algebra = transformed relation.]

### Core Permission Earned

Algebra is source-bound symbolic transformation.

### Not Yet

We are not letting algebra replace the construction as source authority.

We are not treating symbols as free decoration.

We are not accepting transformations that break the relation they were supposed to carry.

### Source Reference

Appendix A: Theorems 584, 586, 587 - source-bound symbolic transformation and equality-preserving form change.

---
# Closing of the Main Path - What Stayed

The book began with one fixed opening.

At first, that was all.

The compass point could stay. The pencil could move. The opening did not change.

Then the opening swept a boundary.

A point on the boundary became a station.

A station made a step.

A step made a triangle.

The step repeated.

The sixth step returned to the start.

Six triangles met around the center.

Their outside edge gave the hexagon.

The hexagon showed three directions.

The directions continued.

The field grew.

Nothing in that path needed to arrive as a rule from nowhere.

Each new idea had a place to stand.

Geometry began when we read figures the field had already made.

Area began when we counted cells the field had already supplied.

Rightness began when selected connectors met cleanly inside the field.

Ratio began when the sides of a field-right triangle could be compared.

Trigonometry began when those ratios received names.

Coordinates arrived later as records.

They helped us check positions, movements, distances, and directions.

Algebra arrived later too.

It remembered relations in symbols.

It let those symbols change form while the relation stayed true.

That is the long echo of the first opening.

At the beginning, the compass could move, but the opening stayed the same.

At the end, symbols can move, but the truth must stay the same.

The point of this book is not that every part of mathematics comes from one little drawing in a simple way.

The point is more careful.

A small construction can carry a surprising amount of memory.

If we keep the order honest, the reader can see where ideas come from.

Look first.

Name later.

Record only what has been earned.

Transform only what can survive the move.

That is the main path.

The appendices now keep the audit trail.

They hold the source ledger, the dependency index, the diagram inventory, and the working notes.

The main chapters have done their job if the reader can return to the picture and say:

I can see where that came from.

---

The trigonometry branch begins with native rightness, then reads right-triangle packets, ratio before name, sine, cosine, tangent, and later unit-circle organization.

The coordinate-geometry branch begins after the field exists. Coordinate labels record stations, differences record movement, norms audit distance, projections organize components, and coordinate work checks constructed relations.

---

## Branch Map — Algebra

The algebra branch begins with symbolic memory. Equality records relations, unknowns mark missing parts, equations hold constraints, lawful transformations preserve truth, formulas compress relations, and solving returns values to the picture.
