**Claim:** Same constructional route differs from same visual appearance.

The same constructional route is not the same thing as the same visual appearance. A figure may look similar without being made by the same lawful steps, and two constructions may preserve the same route even when their placement or outline presentation differs. The proof therefore gives priority to the route. What was selected, swept, stepped, joined, cut, or recomposed must be named before appearance is trusted. What stands is route-over-appearance discipline. The book can now refuse diagrammatic persuasion more strongly: a visual match does not prove a constructional match unless the source route is preserved.

---

### Theorem 222 — Invariant What Permitted Change Does Destroy

**Claim:** An invariant is what a permitted change does not destroy.

Invariant language now becomes explicit. A change may be permitted only when the proof can say what it leaves standing. If a movement, rearrangement, rotation, reflection, translation, or scaling destroys the relation being claimed, the change cannot carry that claim. The invariant is the surviving relation under the permitted change. This theorem does not open every transformation at once; it gives the test by which transformations will be read. What becomes available is invariant-through-change: a controlled way to decide what a change preserves, what it alters, and what it therefore may or may not prove.

---

### Theorem 223 — Transformation Permitted Change Read Through Invariant

**Claim:** A transformation is a permitted change read through an invariant.

Transformation now receives a source-bound definition. It is not any motion, redraw, or symbolic operation the imagination permits. It is a permitted change read through a named invariant. The proof asks both halves to stand: the change must be lawful, and the invariant must be preserved. Only then may the transformation carry proof. This keeps transformation language from becoming a shortcut around construction. What crystallises is transformation-as-controlled-change. Later rotations, reflections, translations, scalings, and rearrangements can be used only where the preserved relation has been named and checked.

---

### Theorem 224 — Rotation Changes Placement While Preserving Station Cycle

**Claim:** Rotation changes placement while preserving the station cycle.

Rotation enters as a controlled change of placement around a cycle. The sixfold station order has already been earned, and a turn-slot can be carried to another turn-slot when the cycle relation is preserved. The proof does not import rotation groups or angle measure. It says only that placement around the station cycle may change while the cycle order and relevant station relations remain standing. What becomes available is native rotation reading: the field can inspect a rotational change by asking whether the station cycle survives. Every stronger rotational theorem waits for its own source payment.

---

### Theorem 225 — Reflection Changes Orientation While Preserving Selected Relations

**Claim:** Reflection changes orientation while preserving selected relations.

Reflection is admitted as a change of orientation under preserved relations. A reflected reading may reverse handedness or face a carrier the other way, but it must keep the selected station relations, lengths, or cell accounts that the proof intends to spend. The theorem does not import mirror symmetry as a ready-made doctrine. It uses invariant discipline: orientation may change; named relations must remain. What stands is native reflection reading, narrow and useful. The field may compare a reversed arrangement with its source only where the preserved relations have been identified.

---

### Theorem 226 — Translation Changes Location While Preserving Local Relation

**Claim:** Translation changes location while preserving local relation.

Translation now becomes a field-readable change of location. A local relation may be carried from one station setting to another when every relevant station role and difference is preserved. The absolute location changes; the local relation remains. This is not a free vector-space operation imposed on the field. It is transportability expressed as a common shift in lawful station records. What becomes available is native translation reading: the proof can move a relation through the substrate only when the local pattern survives the change of place.

---

### Theorem 227 — Scaling Changes Size While Preserving Ratio Pattern

**Claim:** Scaling changes size while preserving ratio pattern.

Scaling is admitted only through ratio pattern. If a body changes size, the proof may not claim full sameness. It may claim a preserved ratio pattern only where corresponding relations remain proportional under an earned rule. This theorem is deliberately cautious. It does not import similarity theory, real-number scaling, or continuous enlargement. It names the invariant that would have to survive: ratio pattern rather than length itself. What becomes available is a controlled scaling distinction. Later ratio arguments may use it, but exact length, area, and station identity must not be silently carried through size change.

---

### Theorem 228 — Rearrangement Changes Outline While Preserving Accounted Parts

**Claim:** Rearrangement changes outline while preserving accounted parts.

Rearrangement now receives its own invariant. Pieces may be moved into a different outline, but a lawful rearrangement must keep the accounted parts present and unchanged. The proof does not let a new outline prove area by appearance. It asks whether the same pieces are still there, neither stretched nor lost nor duplicated nor improperly overlapped. What becomes available is rearrangement-under-accounting. A body may change outline while preserving its parts record. Later area reasoning can use this, but only when the account of pieces remains exact.

---

### Theorem 229 — Resemblance Proof Unless Preserved Relation Named

**Claim:** Resemblance is not proof unless the preserved relation is named.

This theorem restates a central refusal at the level of figures. Resemblance may suggest a proof path, but resemblance alone proves nothing. Two figures may look alike while failing to preserve the relation that matters, and two figures may look different while carrying the needed invariant. The proof therefore demands a named preserved relation before resemblance can count. What becomes available is disciplined resemblance: appearance may point the reader toward inspection, but the proof lands only when the invariant is named. Diagrammatic habit is again kept outside the source boundary.

---

### Theorem 230 — Figure Look Different While Preserving Relevant Invariant

**Claim:** A figure may look different while preserving the relevant invariant.

A figure may change its visible presentation while preserving what the proof needs. Its location may change, its orientation may reverse, or its outline may be rearranged, yet a named relation such as length, area count, station pattern, or ratio may remain standing. The theorem separates visual difference from proof difference. The proof asks what invariant survives rather than whether the figure looks the same. What crystallises is permission to trust a preserved relation through changed appearance. Later transformations can therefore be useful, provided they carry the relevant invariant and do not smuggle in any other sameness.

---

### Theorem 231 — Figure Look Similar While Failing Preserve Relevant

**Claim:** A figure may look similar while failing to preserve the relevant invariant.

The converse warning is just as important. A figure may look similar while the relation needed for proof has failed. A length may have changed, an area account may have lost a piece, a station role may no longer match, or a ratio pattern may not be preserved. The proof refuses to let similarity of appearance carry an unverified invariant. What stands is a guard against false sameness. Later arguments must inspect the relation that matters, not the look of the arrangement. The field remains source-bound because every apparent match must answer to a preserved condition.

---

### Theorem 232 — First Invariant Fixed Compass Opening

**Claim:** The first invariant is the fixed compass opening.

The book now looks back to its first invariant. Before there were cells, carriers, coordinates, decompositions, or transformations, there was a compass opening kept unchanged through motion of the point. That preserved opening gave the field its first lawful sameness. The theorem names it as the root pattern for later invariants: something may change, but the relation that matters remains. What becomes available is historical discipline inside the proof. Later invariant language must remember its ancestor in the fixed opening, where proof first learned to separate permitted movement from forbidden alteration.

---

### Theorem 233 — Later Invariants Echo Fixedopening Pattern

**Claim:** Later invariants echo the fixed-opening pattern.

Later invariants echo the first fixed-opening pattern. A station may move in the ledger while a difference stays unchanged; a figure may rotate while the cycle order stays intact; pieces may rearrange while the accounted area remains. In each case, the proof repeats the same grammar: some feature changes, some named relation is preserved. The theorem does not claim all invariants are the same object. It claims they share the original discipline. What stands is an invariant pattern running through the substrate, allowing later domains to grow without forgetting the first lawful preservation.

---

### Theorem 234 — Substrate Becomes Readable Layers Invariants Named

**Claim:** The substrate becomes readable in layers as invariants are named.

The substrate becomes readable by naming what remains standing at each layer. Fixed opening, boundary step, cell relation, carrier direction, station difference, area count, and ratio pattern are different invariants, and each makes a different layer of the field legible. Without naming them, the figure is only a crowded picture. With them, the proof can say which relations survive which changes. What becomes available is layered readability. The substrate is not one blunt object; it is a record of preserved relations, each layer available only after its invariant has been lawfully earned.

---

### Theorem 235 — Identity Relationpattern Preservation Inside Substrate

**Claim:** Identity is relation-pattern preservation inside the substrate.

Identity now becomes a relation-pattern claim. To say that a body, cell, path, or algebraic record has kept its identity is to say that the relevant pattern of relations has been preserved. The proof refuses identity as mere naming or visual persistence. A named instance can change its look, and a similar-looking instance can fail to carry identity. What matters is the preserved relation pattern inside the substrate. What crystallises is source-bound identity: a thing remains the same for proof only in the respect whose relations survive. This will govern decomposition, recomposition, and symbolic memory alike.

---

### Theorem 236 — Recurrence Permits Assembly

**Claim:** Recurrence permits assembly.

Assembly becomes lawful because recurrence has made parts compatible. A single cell can stand, but repeated cell relations, shared sides, carrier families, and station arrays let cells gather into larger bodies. The proof does not assemble by visual massing. It assembles where repeated relations allow parts to meet without losing their source evidence. What becomes available is recurrence-based assembly. The field can now build bounded wholes from lawful parts, but the right to assemble remains tied to shared sides, preserved station roles, and repeated constructional relations.

---

### Theorem 237 — Assembled Body Gathers Lawful Parts Bounded Whole

**Claim:** An assembled body gathers lawful parts into a bounded whole.

An assembled body is now more than a pile of cells. It gathers lawful parts into a bounded whole. Each part must have a source relation; the parts must meet through accounted sides or stations; and the boundary must mark the body as one readable object. The proof does not import polygon theory or area theory as complete packages. It gives the field a native body: a whole made from pieces whose relations can still be named. What becomes available is assembled-body reading, the basis for later decomposition, area count, and shape comparison.

---

### Theorem 238 — Body Have Boundary Interior Cells Diagonals Heights

**Claim:** A body may have boundary, interior, cells, diagonals, heights, and line-family cuts.

Once a body stands, its internal readings can be separated. It may have a boundary that marks its outer account, interior cells that fill it, diagonals joining non-neighbouring stations, heights following right-meeting or carrier-supported relations, and cuts along native line families. The proof does not grant all these at once for every drawing. It names the possible readings that an assembled body may support when the relevant station relations are present. What becomes available is body anatomy under source control: boundary, interior, cell, diagonal, height, and cut language may enter only where the substrate supplies them.

---

### Theorem 239 — Assembly Opens Decomposition

**Claim:** Assembly opens decomposition.

Assembly now opens the possibility of decomposition. A body made from lawful parts can be read back into parts, because the proof has kept track of how those parts meet. Decomposition is not destruction and not arbitrary cutting. It is a second reading of an assembled whole through relations already present: cell boundaries, shared sides, diagonals, heights, or carrier families. What becomes available is decomposition as a lawful return from whole to accounted parts. Later area and recomposition arguments can use it, but only because assembly preserved a record of the pieces.

---

### Theorem 240 — Decomposition Respect Lawful Substrate Relations

**Claim:** Decomposition must respect lawful substrate relations.

Decomposition receives its rule. A body may be cut or read into pieces only along relations the substrate supplies. If the proposed cut follows an edge, diagonal, height, line family, or cell boundary, it may be inspected. If it ignores the field's station and carrier structure, it has no source authority. The proof therefore refuses arbitrary partitioning. What stands is lawful decomposition: the whole can be divided only where the division is carried by constructional relations. This keeps later area reasoning honest, because the pieces remain proof pieces rather than visual fragments.

---

### Theorem 241 — Lawful Cut Follow Edge

**Claim:** A lawful cut may follow an edge.

The first lawful cut follows an edge. An edge is already a station-to-station relation supplied by the body, often as a unit side or boundary segment. Cutting along it does not invent a new relation; it exposes one that is already present. The proof therefore allows an edge cut as a legitimate decomposition move. This is narrow but important. What becomes available is edge-following decomposition: a body may be separated along a constructed side while preserving the accounted pieces on either side. Cuts that do not follow earned relations remain refused.

---

### Theorem 242 — Lawful Cut Follow Diagonal

**Claim:** A lawful cut may follow a diagonal.

A diagonal cut is lawful only when the diagonal itself is supplied. It must join named stations of the assembled body, and those stations must belong to the body's source record. The proof does not let a diagonal appear because it would be convenient for a later formula. Where the station pair has been earned, the connector can divide the body into accounted parts. What becomes available is diagonal-following decomposition. It gives the body an internal cut relation while preserving the rule that every cut must answer to constructed stations rather than visual desire.

---

### Theorem 243 — Lawful Cut Follow Height

**Claim:** A lawful cut may follow a height.

A height cut may enter only where the body has earned the needed perpendicular or right-meeting relation. The proof does not import altitude theory as a finished subject. It asks for a station, a base or carrier relation, and a lawful right-meeting path from one to the other. When those relations stand, the cut may follow the height. What becomes available is height-following decomposition under native rightness. Later area arguments may use it, but only where the substrate has supplied the height as a proof-bearing relation.

---

### Theorem 244 — Lawful Cut Follow Line Family

**Claim:** A lawful cut may follow a line family.

A carrier line family can support a cut through a body. The cut is lawful when it follows a native direction family and meets the body's stations or edges through relations already supplied. The proof does not draw an arbitrary straight line over the body. It uses the field's own carrier structure as the cutting guide. What becomes available is line-family decomposition: a body can be divided along recurring native direction. This permission will matter for rearrangement and coordinate reading, but it remains limited to cuts grounded in the substrate's carrier families.

---

### Theorem 245 — Lawful Cut Follow Cell Boundary

**Claim:** A lawful cut may follow a cell boundary.

A cell boundary cut is the most local decomposition of an assembled body. If the body is made from cells, the boundary of a cell is already part of the proof record. Cutting along that boundary separates accounted cells without changing the cells themselves. The proof therefore permits a body to be read back through its cellular seams. What becomes available is cell-boundary decomposition. It prepares area as counted cells and recomposition as accounted pieces, while forbidding cuts that would slice through cells without an earned relation to support the division.

---

### Theorem 246 — Decomposition Changes How Body Read Without Destroying

**Claim:** Decomposition changes how a body is read without destroying the body.

Decomposition changes the reading of a body, not the source of its pieces. The body may be viewed as one bounded whole or as several accounted parts along lawful cuts. If the cuts follow substrate relations, the original pieces remain intelligible and the body is not destroyed as proof. The theorem keeps decomposition from becoming erasure. What becomes available is dual readability: whole and parts can both be held when the division is lawful. Later recomposition can move from this account, but it must preserve the pieces that decomposition has made explicit.

---

### Theorem 247 — Recomposition Preserves Proof Pieces Remain Accounted For

**Claim:** Recomposition preserves proof when the same pieces remain accounted for.

Recomposition now receives its positive rule. If a body is decomposed into lawful pieces, those pieces may be reassembled without losing proof only when the same pieces remain accounted for. The proof tracks them through the change: no piece may disappear, stretch, duplicate, or overlap improperly. The new outline may differ, but the account must remain intact. What becomes available is recomposition under accounting. This is the field's first disciplined route toward area preservation: not visual rearrangement, but carried pieces whose identities and counts remain standing.

---

### Theorem 248 — Lawful Recomposition Stretch Pieces

**Claim:** Lawful recomposition must not stretch pieces.

The first recomposition refusal concerns stretching. A piece that has been counted or cut from a body may not be altered in size or internal relation and still be treated as the same piece. Stretching changes the relations that made the piece proof-bearing. The proof therefore refuses any recomposition that preserves appearance of a piece while changing its measure or cell account. What stands is non-stretching discipline. A piece may be moved or reoriented under lawful conditions, but its source relations must not be distorted if area or identity is to be preserved.

---

### Theorem 249 — Lawful Recomposition Lose Pieces

**Claim:** Lawful recomposition must not lose pieces.

A lawful recomposition must not lose pieces. Once decomposition has accounted for parts, every part must appear in the recomposed body if the proof claims preservation. The theorem refuses a common visual cheat: a cleaner outline does not justify a missing piece. The account of parts has priority over the attractiveness of the result. What becomes available is no-loss recomposition discipline. Later area preservation may rely on rearrangement only when every decomposed piece is still present and identifiable inside the new body.

---

### Theorem 250 — Lawful Recomposition Duplicate Pieces

**Claim:** Lawful recomposition must not duplicate pieces.

A lawful recomposition must also refuse duplication. A piece counted once in the decomposition cannot be spent twice in the recomposed body. If it is duplicated, the account changes, even if the outline appears persuasive. The proof therefore separates reuse of a relation from duplication of a piece: a relation may recur under transport, but an accounted piece cannot be counted again as if it were new. What stands is no-duplication discipline. Area and recomposition arguments can proceed only when each piece appears exactly as its account permits.

---

### Theorem 251 — Lawful Recomposition Overlap Pieces Improperly

**Claim:** Lawful recomposition must not overlap pieces improperly.

Improper overlap is the final recomposition refusal. Two pieces may touch along lawful boundaries, but if one covers part of another while both are still counted in full, the account becomes false. The proof therefore demands clean placement or properly accounted overlap. It does not let a compact-looking recomposition hide double counting. What becomes available is no-improper-overlap discipline. The pieces may form a new body only when their contacts, boundaries, and interiors are honestly accounted. This keeps recomposition tied to proof rather than diagrammatic convenience.

---

### Theorem 252 — Area Begins Counting Lawful Cells

**Claim:** Area begins as counting lawful cells.

Area now begins in its most native form: counting lawful cells. The substrate already has an equal-opening triangular cell and assembled bodies made from such cells. Before formulas or units of square measure enter, the proof can count how many lawful cells fill a body. This is not yet mature area theory. It is a source-bound area seed: amount of body read through accounted cells. What becomes available is cell-count area. Later recomposition and formula work may compress the count, but the count begins with cells that the field has actually supplied.

---

### Theorem 253 — Equilateral Cell Native Area Seed

**Claim:** The equilateral cell is the native area seed.

The equal-opening triangular cell becomes the native seed of area. It is not chosen arbitrarily; it is the first cell whose sides were all witnessed by the fixed opening and whose recurrence built the triangular field. Counting area can therefore begin from this cell because the substrate knows how to make it, repeat it, share it, and assemble it. What crystallises is the equilateral cell as area seed. Later bodies may be compared by how many such lawful cells they contain, while imported square units and continuous area formulas remain later translations.

---

### Theorem 254 — Rhombus Be Read Two Equilateral Cells

**Claim:** A rhombus can be read as two equilateral cells.

The rhombus receives a cell reading before any formula is used. Two adjacent equal-opening triangular cells can share a side and together form a four-sided body with opposite sides carried by the triangular field. The proof does not import parallelogram area or rhombus doctrine. It reads the body as two accounted equilateral cells. This gives the rhombus its native area account: two cell seeds joined lawfully. What becomes available is a two-cell rhombus reading, a compact assembled body whose area rests on cell count rather than external measurement.

---

### Theorem 255  -  Hexagon Be Read Six Equilateral Cells

**Claim:** A hexagon can be read as six equilateral cells.

Theorem 255 is a late source-ledger reading of the hexagon as six equilateral cells. By this point the proof no longer has to invent the field from nothing: the fixed opening, boundary stations, equal-opening steps, central cells, and sixfold turn have all been earned. The theorem gathers those earlier permissions and reads the hexagonal body through them. Each central cell is equilateral by equal-opening evidence, and the six cells meet around the center as the completed turn record. The hexagon can therefore be read as six source-earned equilateral cells, not as a school figure recognized by sight. What closes here is the source field's own internal readability.

---

## Part III  -  The Post-255 Bridge

The bridge records the active permissions after Theorem 255 and before the fuse theorems begin.

### 255.A  -  Source ledger completion gate

The bridge begins by treating Theorems 1 through 255 as a completed source record. This does not mean that later proof may take anything it likes from the finished-looking figure. It means that the source ledger has now become inspectable as a connected field of earned permissions. Each later claim must still ask what earlier blocks actually made available. The completion gate therefore turns the first 255 theorems into a usable record without loosening the discipline that produced them. What becomes available is source-ledger reference. What remains refused is retrospective import: no later domain may read its mature concepts back into the beginning.

---

### 255.B  -  Active affordance register

The active affordance register names the permissions that may now be spent. The source ledger is no longer merely a sequence of past proofs; it is a store of lawful capacities. Preserved opening, boundary stationhood, stepping, central cells, turn field, direction families, coordinate audit, native records, and transportability are all available only in the forms in which they were earned. The register keeps memory honest. It lets later proof move quickly without pretending that speed is freedom. What crystallises is a disciplined permission economy: later theorems may spend registered affordances, but they may not invent new source rights by naming them.

---

### 255.C  -  Proof-bearing figure is active

The proof-bearing figure is now active. The field contains cells whose sides and stations are witnessed by construction, not by appearance. This matters because geometry can now begin without being imported. A figure is proof-bearing when its visible form is backed by source relations that can be named and reused. The central equal-opening cell, the sixfold arrangement, and the extended triangular field give later arguments actual evidence to carry. What crystallises is native figurehood: a drawn form may count in proof only where its constructional relations remain alive. What remains refused is visual persuasion without source witness.

---

### 255.D  -  Cell-assembly reading is active

Cell-assembly reading is now active. The proof may no longer see each triangular cell as isolated, because earlier work has shown how cells share sides, preserve directions, and continue through the field. Yet assembly is not the same as loose accumulation. Cells belong together only when their station relations, common sides, and direction families make them one working arrangement. This bridge block gives later proof permission to read local cells as parts of larger structures. It also protects the field from careless wholes. What becomes available is assembled-cell reasoning. What remains refused is treating any cluster of visible triangles as automatically meaningful.

---

### 255.E  -  Coordinate audit is active but non-generative

Coordinate audit is active, and its boundary is now sharp. The field may use coordinates to remember positions, compare station differences, and check relations already supplied by construction. But the coordinate is not the field. It arrives as a reading of what has been made, not as the maker. This bridge block keeps analytic convenience from becoming source theft. Later proof may use records for distance, span, incidence, and comparison, provided the underlying stations have been earned. What crystallises is coordinate discipline: verification is allowed, generation is refused. The coordinate may read the field, but it may not replace it.

---

### 255.F  -  Native difference records are active

Native difference records become active when two stations have already earned their place in the field. A difference is not an abstract pair of numbers floating above construction; it is a record of how one source station stands relative to another. The field's own recurring directions supply the language in which that comparison can be made. This bridge block lets later proof speak of movement between earned stations while refusing coordinate fantasy. What becomes available is native difference as an audit record. What remains refused is any difference whose endpoints have not been constructionally supplied.

---

### 255.G  -  Native squared norm is active

Native squared norm becomes active once a native difference has been recorded. The field can now attach a size-record to a movement between earned stations. This does not import Euclidean distance as a background substance. It records span inside the source field by using the field's own audit structure. The squared norm gives later proof a way to compare movements without abandoning constructional origin. What crystallises is native metric memory: a difference may carry a squared-size record when the stations and the difference have already been earned. What remains refused is algebraic distance creating the relation it merely measures.

---

### 255.H  -  Native norm filters imported lengths

The native norm now becomes a gate against imported lengths. A length may enter the source field only when an earned station difference and its squared norm witness it. Otherwise it is only a number brought from elsewhere. This bridge block is one of the great protections of the system: it separates witnessed span from mathematical wish. Later proof may speak of native distances, but only where construction and audit have made them available together. What crystallises is a length filter. Every unwitnessed magnitude, however familiar from school mathematics, remains outside until the field itself supplies it.

---

### 255.I  -  Span availability differs from triangle realization

Span availability is weaker than triangle realization. The field may contain one span here, another span there, and a third somewhere else, but that does not yet make a triangle. A triangle requires a compatible triple of stations joined by compatible spans in one realized figure. This bridge block prevents a subtle but dangerous shortcut: listing lengths is not the same as constructing their meeting. Later right-triangle claims must therefore prove realization, not merely availability. What becomes available is a clean distinction between the span ledger and the triangle ledger. What remains refused is imported triple reasoning detached from field-witnessed station assemblies.

---

### 255.J  -  Native field-rightness is active

Native field-rightness becomes active when the field can audit a companion relation as right-meeting from within its own records. Rightness is not borrowed from a diameter theorem, a square grid, or analytic orthogonality. It appears when earned station differences meet under a native audit that makes the relation stand. This bridge block prepares trigonometry without naming trigonometry. It gives the field its own way to witness perpendicular companionship. What crystallises is native rightness: a source-earned right-meeting relation may now be spent in later ratio arguments. What remains refused is every external right-angle authority not grounded in the field.

---

### 255.K  -  First field-right triangle is 1, root three, 2

The first field-right triangle carries the side pattern one, root three, two. The unit span is already available; the companion span has squared size three times the unit; and the closing side has squared size four. The three sides gather into one native right-triangle packet before any trigonometric name is allowed to appear. This block is not importing a famous triangle. It is showing how the field produces the packet from its own stations, norms, and rightness audit. What becomes available is the first source-earned side-ratio body. What remains refused is sine, cosine, tangent, or any mature trigonometric function before its evidence exists.

---

### 255.L  -  First ratio packet is active before trigonometric naming

The first ratio packet is active before trigonometric naming. Once the one, root three, two triangle stands, its sides may be compared from a chosen acute angle. One side becomes opposite, one adjacent, and the side opposite the right meeting becomes the hypotenuse. The ratios are not yet functions; they are stable comparisons inside an earned figure. This bridge block lets trigonometry begin at the right level of humility. What crystallises is ratio-before-name: later sine, cosine, and tangent may compress these comparisons, but they may not generate them. The proof owns the packet before it owns the vocabulary.

---

### 255.M  -  Root seven is a first oblique stress-test

Root seven becomes the first oblique stress-test when it is not merely recorded as a length but realized as a role inside a field-right triangle. The field can witness spans with squared sizes three, four, and seven; when those spans assemble compatibly, root seven becomes the hypotenuse of an earned right-triangle relation. This matters because a new length is not automatically a new trigonometric object. It must take its place in a realized packet. What crystallises is oblique activation: root seven becomes structurally live only through field-right realization. What remains refused is treating every audited span as already ratio-bearing.

---

### 255.N  -  First appearance is rule-relative

First appearance is rule-relative. The field can be searched or expanded under different lawful procedures, and different procedures may reveal the same object at different depths. A span may appear early under one rule and later under another. This bridge block keeps discovery language honest. It separates the mathematical object from the rule under which it is first encountered. Later proof may speak of first appearance only by naming the expansion or search rule that makes the claim true. What crystallises is emergence discipline. What remains refused is absolute priority language that forgets the procedure by which the field was explored.

---

### 255.O  -  Mini-ladder extraction is permitted

Mini-ladder extraction is now permitted. A later domain does not need to replay the entire source ledger every time it begins. It needs a lawful route through selected permissions. A mini ladder is that route: a small sequence of earned affordances arranged so that a new class of mathematical sentences can land. The danger is thematic borrowing, so the bridge insists that every rung be paid for. Geometry, trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and algebra may now be tested by source-bound ladders. What crystallises is disciplined fuse extraction. What remains refused is beginning from the mature branch and pretending the source has already granted it.

---

### 255.P  -  Fuse claims must spend only registered affordances

The final bridge block states the rule for everything that follows. A fuse theorem remains a theorem; it cannot use the mature language of its target domain as its source. It may spend only registered affordances or permissions earned by earlier fuse work. This is what keeps branch ignition from becoming drift. Geometry, trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and algebra may now begin, but only as consequences of the field, not as authorities imposed on it. What crystallises is the hard discipline of the fuse interval: every later claim must pay its way. The bridge closes by opening the branches under law.

---

## Part IV  -  The Fuse Ledger

Theorems 256-587 are installed here as readable paragraphs showing the ignition of the mathematical branches.

### Theorem 256 — A compass opening can be fixed before construction begins

**Claim:** A compass opening can be fixed before construction begins.

The fuse ledger begins by returning to the first act with branch discipline in mind. Before any figure, axis, coordinate, ratio, or formula is allowed to speak, a compass opening can be fixed. This is not repetition for its own sake. It is the source being made available to later mathematical branches without surrendering authority to them. The opening is fixed before construction begins, so every later sweep must answer to that first preservation. What becomes available for the fuse interval is the same primitive right the source ledger began with: a measure can be held before it is spent.

---

### Theorem 257 — The fixed opening remains the same opening during construction

**Claim:** The fixed opening remains the same opening during construction.

The fixed opening must remain the same while construction proceeds. A point may be placed, a sweep may be made, and a station may later receive further action, but the opening itself is not altered by the work it supports. The fuse ledger needs this restated because every branch will be tempted to use a mature shortcut. Geometry, trigonometry, coordinates, and algebra all begin under the same restraint. What stands is preservation during construction: the opening may serve many acts only because it remains the same opening through them.

---

### Theorem 258 — The compass point may be placed at one station

**Claim:** The compass point may be placed at one station.

Construction needs a place to begin. A station can be selected, and the compass point may be placed there. This act is small, but it gives the fixed opening a local source from which action can proceed. The proof does not yet grant a whole plane of available points, nor any later coordinate freedom. It grants one lawful placement. What becomes available is station-based compass action: the fixed opening can now be used from a selected station, and every later branch must remember that action begins from a placed point, not from abstract space.

---

### Theorem 259 — The compass point may later be moved to another station

**Claim:** The compass point may later be moved to another station.

The compass point may later move to another station, provided the station has been made available. This movement is not a change of measure. It is a change of placement for the same instrument relation. The proof keeps point transfer distinct from opening alteration. That distinction will matter for every branch, because reuse across the field depends on moving the source of action without losing what was fixed. What becomes available is station transfer: the compass can act from more than one lawful station while preserving the opening that gives the action its measure.

---

### Theorem 260 — Moving the compass point does not alter the fixed opening

**Claim:** Moving the compass point does not alter the fixed opening.

The point's movement is now separated from the opening's preservation. When the compass point is moved to another station, the opening is not thereby widened, narrowed, or redefined. The proof refuses a hidden change of measure inside a change of location. This is one of the deep safeguards of the whole book. Later transport, congruence-like reading, coordinate audit, and algebraic memory all depend on it. What crystallises is transfer without alteration: the compass may move, but the fixed opening remains the invariant carried through the move.

---

### Theorem 261 — A sweep made after station transfer uses the same fixed opening

**Claim:** A sweep made after station transfer uses the same fixed opening.

After station transfer, a sweep can still spend the same opening. The compass point now rests at the new station, but the pencil point reaches according to the preserved measure. The proof does not treat this as a new radius or a new unit. It is the same fixed opening acting from a new lawful place. What becomes available is post-transfer sweep authority. A later boundary, step, or cell may arise from the transferred station only because the sweep uses the opening already preserved through the move.

---

### Theorem 262 — Compass action is reusable after station transfer

**Claim:** Compass action is reusable after station transfer.

Compass action is now reusable after station transfer. The field is not confined to a single center or a single sweep. Once the opening survives movement of the compass point, the same kind of action can be performed from another lawful station. The proof does not yet create all figures; it creates reusability under a preserved relation. This is the small engine beneath the later branches. What becomes available is repeated constructional action: a fixed opening can be spent again from a new station, while every use remains tied to the opening that was first fixed.

---

### Theorem 263 — A center station can be selected

**Claim:** A center station can be selected.

A center station can now be selected from the available field of lawful stationhood. The word center does not yet carry the whole theory of circles. It names the station from which the next sweep will be organized. The proof therefore gives centerhood as a role, not as imported geometry. A station becomes a center when selected to hold the compass point for a sweep. What becomes available is center-station authority: later boundary generation may begin from this selected role, while every unselected or unearned point remains outside the construction.

---

### Theorem 264 — The fixed opening is available before the sweep

**Claim:** The fixed opening is available before the sweep.

Before a sweep begins, the fixed opening is already available. This order is essential. The sweep does not define the measure after the fact; it spends a measure that has already been preserved. The proof keeps the source relation prior to the visible trace. That priority protects later circle, boundary, and ratio language from reversing the explanation. What stands is opening-before-sweep discipline: the measure is fixed first, then the action uses it. The branch ledger may now approach boundary generation without pretending the drawn curve is the source of its own measure.

---

### Theorem 265 — The compass point can be placed at the center station

**Claim:** The compass point can be placed at the center station.

The selected center station now receives the compass point. This placement joins two permissions: a station may serve as a place of action, and the fixed opening is ready before the sweep. The proof has not yet drawn the boundary; it has arranged the lawful conditions for drawing it. The compass point stands at the center, the opening remains fixed, and the next act can be a center-bound sweep. What becomes available is the immediate precondition for boundary generation, held without importing any finished circle theory.

---

### Theorem 266 — Pencil Point Held at Fixed Opening

**Claim:** The pencil point can be held at the fixed opening from the center.

The compass is now ready to make the fixed opening visible. The center station has been selected, and the compass point can rest there; the pencil point is held away from it by the already fixed opening. This does not yet draw a boundary or prove a circle. It only arranges the relation that a sweep will spend. The proof keeps the two stations of the instrument distinct: the point anchors the action, while the pencil point carries the preserved reach. What becomes available is center-bound reach before motion, the immediate condition under which a boundary trace can lawfully appear.

---

### Theorem 267 — Compass Sweep Under Fixed Opening

**Claim:** The compass can sweep while the opening remains fixed.

A sweep may now occur without changing the measure it spends. The compass point stays at the center station, the pencil point remains held at the fixed opening, and the hand can turn the instrument. Motion is allowed, but alteration is not. That restraint is the whole proof pressure of this block. The trace that follows will matter only because the opening does not change during the sweep. What crystallises is sweep-with-preservation: the compass may move around the center while the reach remains the same. The later boundary therefore begins as a moving act under one fixed relation, not as an imported circle concept.

---

### Theorem 268 — Reached Positions Keep Center Opening

**Claim:** Each reached position keeps the fixed opening from the center station.

As the sweep proceeds, each reached position must answer to the same center-bound relation. A reached mark is not merely a visible point on the page; it is a position contacted by the pencil point while the compass point remains at the center and the opening remains fixed. The proof therefore gathers reached positions by their witness: each is held from the center by the preserved opening. No equation, radius theory, or complete point-set doctrine has entered. What becomes available is a class of reached positions all carrying the same source relation to the center station.

---

### Theorem 269 — Reached Positions Form Boundary Around Center

**Claim:** The reached positions form a boundary around the center station.

The many reached positions can now be read together. Each has been contacted by the sweep under the fixed opening from the center, so they are not scattered marks. They gather as a boundary around the center station because one preserved reach organizes them. The proof does not need to import the full language of circles. It only says that the sweep has left a center-bound trace. What stands is boundary formation from repeated reached positions under a single opening, a visible field edge whose authority comes from construction rather than from later geometry.

---

### Theorem 270 — Fixed Sweep Generates Base Boundary

**Claim:** The fixed sweep generates a base boundary.

The first boundary now receives its source name. A selected center, a fixed opening, and a sweep held under that opening have produced a boundary around the center. This is the base boundary because it is the first field made from the original compass act. The proof refuses to let the boundary be treated as a free circle from a textbook. It is generated here, by this sweep, under this opening. What becomes available is the base boundary as a lawful object of later selection, stepping, and station work.

---

### Theorem 271 — Generated Base Boundary Present

**Claim:** A base boundary has already been generated around the center.

The next movement begins by remembering what is already present. The base boundary does not need to be redrawn or re-earned in a new language. It has been generated around the center by the fixed sweep, and it may now serve as a source condition for later action. This theorem is a memory gate: the proof may use the base boundary because it has a receipt. What becomes available is boundary presence for selection. What remains refused is treating some other ungenerated curve, imagined circle, or loose visual edge as if it had the same authority.

---

### Theorem 272 — Boundary Position Selection

**Claim:** A position may be selected on that boundary.

A boundary can now offer a position, but selection must be explicit. The proof does not release every visible point at once. It chooses a position on the generated base boundary and allows that position to become available for the next act. The restriction matters because later stationhood depends on source selection, not on a vague continuum of possible locations. What crystallises is boundary selection: a reached position on the generated boundary can be picked out for further use. What remains outside is unselected point freedom.

---

### Theorem 273 — Selected Boundary Position as Station

**Claim:** The selected boundary position can be treated as a station.

The selected boundary position now receives a stronger role. It is not only a place reached by the sweep; once selected, it may be treated as a station. Stationhood means the proof can refer to it, place action there, and preserve its relation to earlier construction. The step is small but decisive: the boundary begins to return usable points to the construction. What becomes available is a selected boundary station, a lawful place from which further compass work may proceed. Nothing here grants all boundary positions the same status without selection.

---

### Theorem 274 — Compass Point Placed at Boundary Station

**Claim:** The compass point may be placed at that station.

The compass point can now move from the original center to the selected boundary station. This movement spends the earlier permission that station transfer does not alter the opening. The proof separates the new placement from any change in measure. The boundary station receives the compass point because it has been selected and made station-like by construction. What becomes available is action from a boundary station. The original center is not erased, and the opening is not redefined; the construction simply gains a second lawful place of compass action.

---

### Theorem 275 — Fixed Opening Available at Boundary Station

**Claim:** The fixed opening remains available at that station.

After the compass point is placed at the boundary station, the fixed opening remains available there. This theorem protects the transfer from smuggling in a new measure. The opening that generated the base boundary is the same opening now carried to the selected station. The proof therefore preserves the invariant while allowing the source of action to change location. What becomes available is boundary-station reach under the original opening. Later stepping can spend this relation, but only because the measure has survived placement at the new station.

---

### Theorem 276 — Boundary-Station Sweep Uses Fixed Opening

**Claim:** A sweep from that station uses the fixed opening.

A sweep from the boundary station may now be made under the preserved opening. The compass point rests at the selected boundary station, and the pencil point reaches according to the same measure carried from the first construction. The proof does not create a second independent circle theory here. It permits one local sweep from a lawfully selected station. What crystallises is a boundary-station sweep using the original opening, a new action whose authority is inherited from preservation rather than from visual expectation.

---

### Theorem 277 — Boundary Station Supports Later Compass Action

**Claim:** The station supports later compass action.

The selected boundary station has now become more than a marker on the first boundary. It has received the compass point, retained the fixed opening, and supported a sweep. Those acts show that it can support later compass action. The proof gathers the permissions without widening them beyond their source. A boundary station supports action because it was selected, made a station, and used under the preserved opening. What becomes available is boundary-station agency for later construction; what remains refused is an automatic right for every unselected position to act.

---

### Theorem 278 — Selected Boundary Positions Serve as Compass Stations

**Claim:** Selected boundary positions can serve as compass stations.

The local result can now be stated as a controlled general permission over selected cases. A position on the base boundary, once selected, may serve as a compass station. The proof does not say all boundary positions are already active, nor does it replace selection with visual membership. It says selected boundary positions can receive compass action under the fixed opening. What stands is a usable rule for station formation on the base boundary. Later boundary stepping can proceed because the boundary is no longer only a trace; it is a source of selected stations.

---

### Theorem 279 — Base Boundary Present for Stepping

**Claim:** The base boundary is present.

Stepping begins by returning to the base boundary. The proof marks its presence because the next act needs a boundary to meet, not merely a station from which to sweep. The boundary was generated by the fixed sweep from the center, and that generated object remains in the field. This theorem is therefore a source reminder: the stepping argument has somewhere to land. What becomes available is the base boundary as the target of a later station sweep. No general intersection theory or full circle doctrine has been imported.

---

### Theorem 280 — Boundary Station Selected for Step

**Claim:** A boundary station can be selected on the base boundary.

A station for stepping can now be selected on the base boundary. Selection again prevents the proof from treating the entire boundary as already active. A particular boundary position is chosen and held as a station from which the compass may act. The theorem prepares the first measured passage along the boundary by naming the start of that passage. What becomes available is a selected boundary station for stepping, with all later reach still tied to the fixed opening and the generated boundary.

---

### Theorem 281 — Compass Point at Selected Boundary Station

**Claim:** The compass point can be placed at the selected boundary station.

The selected boundary station now receives the compass point. This act repeats the transfer discipline in the service of stepping. The compass is not changing its opening; it is changing where the fixed opening acts from. Because the station was selected on the base boundary, the placement is lawful. What becomes available is a sweep source on the boundary itself. The next movement can search for a meeting with the base boundary without pretending that the boundary station generated its own authority.

---

### Theorem 282 — Opening Unchanged at Selected Boundary Station

**Claim:** The fixed opening remains unchanged at that station.

At the selected boundary station the opening remains unchanged. This block prevents a silent measure shift exactly where the proof is most vulnerable to it. The same opening that reached from the center is now present at the boundary station. The station is new as a place of action, but the measure is not new. What crystallises is unchanged opening after boundary placement. Later claims about equal-opening steps may spend this preservation, and only this preservation, as their source witness.

---

### Theorem 283 — Sweep From Selected Boundary Station

**Claim:** A sweep from the selected boundary station uses the fixed opening.

A sweep from the selected boundary station can now be made using the unchanged opening. The proof allows motion from the new station while keeping the original measure active. This sweep is not free drawing and not a new theory of circle intersection. It is a local action under a preserved relation. What becomes available is the boundary-station sweep as a candidate for meeting the base boundary. Later stepping will depend on this controlled sweep, not on a visual guess about where the next station should be.

---

### Theorem 284 — Boundary-Station Sweep May Meet Base Boundary

**Claim:** That sweep may meet the base boundary.

The sweep from the boundary station is now allowed to meet the base boundary. The proof holds two generated traces in relation: the already present base boundary and the new sweep made from the selected boundary station with the fixed opening. Where they meet, the construction can identify a position. The theorem does not inflate this into a general intersection doctrine. It gives the local meeting needed for stepping. What becomes available is a meeting between a station-sweep and the base boundary, the first material for the next boundary station.

---

### Theorem 285 — Meeting Point as Another Boundary Station

**Claim:** A meeting point of the sweep and the base boundary is another boundary station.

When the boundary-station sweep meets the base boundary, the meeting point carries two pieces of evidence. It lies on the base boundary, and it is reached from the selected station by the fixed opening. The proof may therefore treat it as another boundary station, once the meeting is selected by construction. This is not arbitrary point creation; it is stationhood born from a double witness. What becomes available is the next boundary station in the stepping process, a place earned by meeting rather than guessed by eye.

---

### Theorem 286 — Step Uses Fixed Opening

**Claim:** The step from the selected station to the meeting station uses the fixed opening.

The relation between the selected station and the new meeting station is now named as a step. The new station was reached by the sweep from the selected boundary station, and that sweep used the fixed opening. Therefore the passage from one station to the other carries the same opening as its witness. The proof does not yet claim a whole polygon or a closed circuit. It earns one measured step along the boundary. What becomes available is a fixed-opening boundary step, ready for repetition only under the same preserved conditions.

---

### Theorem 287 — Equal-Opening Stepping Available

**Claim:** Equal-opening stepping is available along the base boundary.

The local boundary step can now become a repeatable permission. A selected boundary station can support a fixed-opening sweep; the sweep may meet the base boundary; the meeting can become another boundary station; and the station-to-station passage uses the fixed opening. Together these facts make equal-opening stepping available along the base boundary. The proof still refuses closure, regular polygon language, and general chord theory. It owns a step procedure. Later repetition may spend that procedure, but each step must keep the opening and boundary relations that made the first one lawful.

---

### Theorem 288 — Base Boundary From Center

**Claim:** A base boundary is generated from the center station.

The cell argument begins by remembering the base boundary's origin. It was generated from the center station by a fixed-opening sweep. That origin matters because the center-to-boundary relations will become side witnesses. The proof therefore returns to the boundary not as a mere curve but as a record of center-bound reach. What becomes available is the base boundary with its center witness active. Later cells may use the center only because the boundary has kept its relation to that source station.

---

### Theorem 289 — First Boundary Station on Base Boundary

**Claim:** A first boundary station lies on the base boundary.

A first boundary station is selected on the base boundary. Because the boundary was generated from the center, this station is already tied to the center by the fixed opening. The proof does not yet form a cell; it places one of the future cell's boundary supports. The station is lawful because it is selected on the generated boundary. What becomes available is the first boundary station as a side endpoint for later construction, while every unselected boundary location remains inactive.

---

### Theorem 290 — Second Boundary Station Reached From First

**Claim:** A second boundary station is reached from the first boundary station by the fixed opening.

The second boundary station is earned by stepping from the first. The compass point is placed at the first boundary station, the fixed opening remains unchanged, and the sweep reaches the base boundary at another selected position. That reached position becomes the second boundary station. The proof thereby supplies the boundary-to-boundary side of the coming cell. What becomes available is an adjacent boundary station reached by the same opening, not a point chosen because the figure looks convenient.

---

### Theorem 291 — Center Reaches First Station

**Claim:** The center reaches the first boundary station by the fixed opening.

The first side witness of the central cell is now named. Since the first boundary station lies on the base boundary generated from the center, the center reaches it by the fixed opening. This relation is not radius theory imported after the fact; it is the original sweep relation being read at a selected station. What becomes available is the center-to-first-station side witness. The cell will stand only if each side receives such a source-bound witness.

---

### Theorem 292 — Center Reaches Second Station

**Claim:** The center reaches the second boundary station by the fixed opening.

The second boundary station also lies on the base boundary, and the base boundary was generated from the center by the fixed opening. The center therefore reaches the second boundary station by that same opening. The proof now holds a second center-bound side witness, parallel in source to the first but attached to the next station. What becomes available is center-to-second-station evidence. The construction still has not claimed equality in general; it has named a second use of the same opening in this cell.

---

### Theorem 293 — First Station Reaches Second Station

**Claim:** The first boundary station reaches the second boundary station by the fixed opening.

The third side witness comes from the boundary step. The second boundary station was reached from the first by a sweep using the fixed opening. Therefore the first boundary station reaches the second by that opening. The proof now has three station-pair relations: two from the center to boundary stations, and one from boundary station to boundary station. What becomes available is the final side witness needed for a central cell. The triangle is not being assumed; its side evidence is being assembled.

---

### Theorem 294 — Three Station-Pairs Share Opening

**Claim:** The three station-pairs use the same fixed opening.

The proof now compares the three side witnesses. Center to first station, center to second station, and first station to second station have all been carried by the fixed opening. The sameness is not decorative; its invariant is named. The same opening witnesses each station-pair. What becomes available is shared-opening evidence across the three relations. This does not yet license every property of an equilateral triangle, but it gives the cell its source-bound side equality in the only form presently earned.

---

### Theorem 295 — Three Stations Form Cell

**Claim:** The three stations form a three-sided cell.

With the three station-pairs named, the three stations can form a three-sided cell. The center, first boundary station, and second boundary station are held together by the three relations already witnessed. The proof does not rely on the appearance of a triangle; it uses the station-pair structure to make a bounded cell readable. What becomes available is a central three-sided cell, assembled from lawful endpoints and witnessed sides. Later naming may become stronger, but only because this cell has first been made to stand.

---

### Theorem 296 — Cell Sides Witnessed by Same Opening

**Claim:** Each side of the cell is witnessed by the same fixed opening.

The three-sided cell now receives its side reading. Each side corresponds to one of the station-pairs already named, and each station-pair is witnessed by the same fixed opening. The proof gathers those relations into the cell itself. The result is not a general triangle theorem but a precise source condition: this cell's sides share one opening. What becomes available is equal-opening side evidence inside the central cell, a permission strong enough to support native equilateral language and narrow enough to remain honest.

---

### Theorem 297 — Equilateral Language Licensed

**Claim:** The cell is equal-opening, and only then may equilateral language be licensed.

Only now may the familiar word begin to enter. The cell has three sides, and each side is witnessed by the same fixed opening. On that source-bound basis the cell may be called equal-opening, and equilateral language may be licensed for this native case. The proof does not import the full school package of equilateral triangles, angle measures, or congruence tests. It licenses the name because the side evidence has been paid for. What stands is the first branch-facing equilateral permission, grounded in construction rather than in recognition.

---

### Theorem 298 — Base Boundary Present for Closure

**Claim:** The base boundary is present.

The closure argument begins with the base boundary already in the field. The proof cannot close a circuit without a boundary on which steps can land. That boundary was generated from the center by the fixed sweep, so it remains available as the path of equal-opening stepping. This theorem reactivates the setting for repetition. What becomes available is the base boundary as the arena of a boundary walk. No closure, polygon, or symmetry is assumed yet; only the generated boundary is present for stepping.

---

### Theorem 299 — Starting Boundary Station Selected

**Claim:** A starting boundary station is selected.

A boundary walk needs a beginning. The proof selects one boundary station on the base boundary and treats it as the starting station. The selection is not a numbering convention imposed from outside; it is the first station of the constructional walk. From here the fixed opening can be reused step by step. What becomes available is a named start for the boundary circuit. The later return will matter precisely because the proof has fixed where the walk began.

---

### Theorem 300 — Opening Reused From Start

**Claim:** The fixed opening is reused from the starting station.

From the starting station, the fixed opening is reused. The compass point moves to the selected station without altering the opening, and the sweep from that station spends the same measure. The proof thereby begins the boundary walk with preserved evidence rather than arbitrary spacing. What becomes available is the first act of repetition: the original opening can operate from the starting boundary station. Every later step will have to preserve the same discipline.

---

### Theorem 301 — First Step Reaches Next Station

**Claim:** The first step reaches the next boundary station.

The first reuse of the opening reaches the next boundary station. The sweep from the starting station meets the base boundary, and that meeting becomes the next station in the walk. The proof names the result as a step because the station-to-station passage is witnessed by the fixed opening. What becomes available is the first nextness of the boundary cycle: one station leads lawfully to another. No sequence has yet closed, but an ordered walk has begun.

---

### Theorem 302 — Opening Reused From Each Reached Station

**Claim:** The fixed opening is reused from each reached boundary station.

The walk can continue because each reached boundary station can itself support compass action. The fixed opening is reused from the station just reached, then from the next, and so on. The proof does not replace constructional repetition with an abstract rule too early. It insists that each reuse depends on stationhood and preservation. What becomes available is iterative reuse: each newly reached boundary station can become the source of the next fixed-opening step.

---

### Theorem 303 — Each Reuse Produces Next Boundary Station

**Claim:** Each reuse produces the next boundary station on the base boundary.

Each reuse of the fixed opening seeks the base boundary again. When the sweep from a reached station meets that boundary, the meeting becomes the next boundary station. The walk therefore advances by a repeated constructional pattern: station, preserved opening, sweep, boundary meeting, next station. This gives nextness its source-bound form. What becomes available is a repeated station-producing step along the base boundary, not a prewritten polygonal list.

---

### Theorem 304 — Steps Proceed Around Boundary

**Claim:** The equal-opening steps proceed around the base boundary.

The equal-opening steps now proceed around the base boundary. Each station gives rise to the next by the same preserved opening, and the base boundary remains the place of meeting. The proof follows the walk as a sequence of earned steps rather than as a completed figure recognized in advance. What becomes available is around-boundary progression under one measure. The motion is controlled enough to be counted, but not yet closed until the construction reaches its starting station again.

---

### Theorem 305 — Six Steps Complete Circuit

**Claim:** Six equal-opening steps complete the boundary circuit.

The repeated walk now reaches its circuit count. Six equal-opening steps, each made by reuse of the same opening along the base boundary, complete the first boundary circuit. The proof does not import a regular hexagon and then count its sides. It counts the constructional steps themselves. What becomes available is six-step completion as a boundary fact. The number belongs to the source walk, not to an external polygon theory brought in afterwards.

---

### Theorem 306 — Sixth Step Returns to Start

**Claim:** The sixth step returns to the starting boundary station.

The completion becomes closure when the sixth step returns to the starting boundary station. The proof has held the start in memory, followed each equal-opening step, and now sees the walk come back to its beginning. This return is the event that closes the circuit. It does not need angle measure, rotational symmetry, or analytic coordinates. What crystallises is return-after-six: the boundary walk has found its starting station again under the preserved opening.

---

### Theorem 307 — Boundary Stepping Closes

**Claim:** Boundary stepping closes after six equal-opening steps.

The return can now be stated as the closure rule for this source walk. Boundary stepping closes after six equal-opening steps because the sixth step returns to the starting station. The proof gathers the sequence and the return into one permission. What stands is six-step boundary closure, a finite and source-earned result. Later cells, turns, and hexagonal readings may spend this closure, but only as this construction's closure, not as a general polygon law imported from elsewhere.

---

### Theorem 308 — Six-Step Closure Available for Turn Reading

**Claim:** Boundary stepping closes after six equal-opening steps.

The six-step closure is restated at the entrance to turn reading. This repetition is useful because the next theorems will spend closure in a new way: not only as a boundary event but as a central field condition. The proof therefore keeps the closure active and named. Six equal-opening boundary steps return to the start, and that return will govern how cells meet around the center. What becomes available is closure as a live premise for the central turn, while every later angular reading remains downstream of this boundary fact.

---

### Theorem 309 — Adjacent Pair Forms Central Cell

**Claim:** Each adjacent boundary-station pair forms a central cell with the center.

Each adjacent boundary-station pair can now be joined to the center as a central cell. The two center-to-boundary reaches and the boundary-to-boundary step supply the three side witnesses. The proof uses the cell rule already earned: a center and an adjacent boundary pair form a three-sided equal-opening cell. What becomes available is a cell for each adjacent pair, produced by the boundary order rather than by visual subdivision. The around-center structure begins from these local cell formations.

---

### Theorem 310 — First Central Cell Uses First Pair

**Claim:** The first central cell uses the first adjacent pair.

The first central cell is formed from the first adjacent boundary-station pair and the center. This names the beginning of the around-center cell sequence. The proof does not yet claim that all cells fill a turn; it installs the first cell by the side relations already available. What becomes available is the first central cell in order, tied to the starting boundary station and the next station reached by one equal-opening step.

---

### Theorem 311 — Next Cells Use Next Pairs

**Claim:** Each next central cell uses the next adjacent pair.

The sequence of central cells follows the sequence of boundary pairs. After the first pair gives the first cell, each next adjacent pair with the center gives the next central cell. The proof lets the boundary walk order control the cell order. No rotation theory is needed; nextness comes from stepping. What becomes available is a cell sequence around the center, one local cell at a time, each grounded in an adjacent boundary pair.

---

### Theorem 312 — Six Pairs Produce Six Cells

**Claim:** The six adjacent pairs produce six central cells.

The six boundary steps provide six adjacent boundary-station pairs, and each pair forms a central cell with the center. The count is therefore carried from the boundary walk into the central assembly. The proof does not first imagine a six-part turn; it lets six adjacent pairs produce six cells. What becomes available is the six-cell central assembly, a countable body around the center whose parts are sourced by the boundary cycle.

---

### Theorem 313 — Six Cells Meet Around Center

**Claim:** The six central cells meet around the center station.

The six central cells all share the center station, and their boundary pairs follow one another around the closed boundary cycle. The proof can therefore read them as meeting around the center. Their meeting is not a visual flourish; it follows from shared centerhood and ordered adjacency. What becomes available is around-center assembly: six cells with one common center and consecutive boundary supports. This prepares turn language without importing degrees.

---

### Theorem 314 — Final Cell Returns to Start

**Claim:** The closure step returns the final cell to the starting boundary station.

The final central cell is controlled by the closure step. The sixth boundary step returns to the starting boundary station, so the last adjacent pair closes the cell sequence back to the beginning. The proof thereby prevents an open spiral of cells. The final cell is not extra decoration; it is the cell forced by the closing boundary pair. What becomes available is a closed central cell sequence, with the last cell tied back to the starting station by the source walk.

---

### Theorem 315 — No Further Cell Needed

**Claim:** No further central cell is needed to complete the circuit.

Once the final cell returns to the starting boundary station, no further central cell is needed for the first circuit. The proof makes a refusal: after closure, adding another cell would not complete an unmet demand; it would begin a new passage or duplicate the closed one. The central assembly is therefore complete as a six-cell circuit. What becomes available is a bounded turn reading, while any seventh-cell requirement is refused unless a new constructional demand is separately introduced.

---

### Theorem 316 — Six Cells Fill Complete Turn

**Claim:** The six central cells fill one complete turn around the center.

The six central cells now fill one complete turn around the center. This conclusion is earned by the sequence: six adjacent boundary pairs, six central cells, common center, and closure back to the starting station. The proof does not measure the turn in degrees. It lets the source circuit define completion. What stands is a complete central turn made of six equal-opening cells. Later branch language may call on this turn, but its authority remains the closed six-cell assembly.

---

### Theorem 317 — Central Turn Field Available

**Claim:** A central turn field is available.

The completed six-cell assembly can now be named a central turn field. A field is available because the cells are not isolated; they meet around the center, close without a gap, and preserve their side witnesses. The theorem gathers the previous work into a usable permission. What becomes available is the central turn field as a source object for later direction, angle, ratio, and decomposition readings. What remains refused is any mature theory of angular measure that forgets how this turn was constructed.

---

### Theorem 318 — Six Stations Present After Closure

**Claim:** Six boundary stations are present after closure.

After closure, six boundary stations stand in the record. They are not a generic set of points on a circle; they are the stations produced by equal-opening stepping from the selected start and returned to by the sixth step. The proof may now use their presence for connection and direction work. What becomes available is the six-station boundary register. Later connections among these stations must still be selected and justified, but the stations themselves are now present as products of the closed boundary walk.

---

### Theorem 319 — Adjacent Stations Joined by Steps

**Claim:** Adjacent boundary stations may be joined by boundary steps.

Adjacent boundary stations may now be joined by the steps that produced them. Each neighbouring pair in the boundary order is connected by one fixed-opening reach. The proof does not need a general chord theorem to say this. It simply remembers the stepping relation between successive stations. What becomes available is adjacent station connection by earned boundary step, a local relation that later direction-family readings may compare across the sixfold field.

---

### Theorem 320 — Selected Nonadjacent Connections

**Claim:** Nonadjacent stations may be connected when construction selects them.

Nonadjacent stations can be connected only when construction selects them for comparison. The proof refuses to draw every possible connector at once. It allows a station-to-station connection when the relation is needed and the endpoints are already present. This creates a controlled way to inspect longer bearings inside the sixfold field. What becomes available is selected nonadjacent connection, while arbitrary web-making and unearned line theory remain outside the source boundary.

---

### Theorem 321 — Same Direction Among Connections

**Claim:** Some station-to-station connections preserve the same direction.

Once station-to-station connections are selected, their bearings can be compared. Some of those connections preserve the same direction as other connections elsewhere in the field. The proof does not yet call this full parallelism, nor does it import slope or angle criteria. It only records sameness of bearing where the constructed connections display it. What becomes available is same-direction evidence among selected station connections, the first material for direction-family formation.

---

### Theorem 322 — Direction Recurs Elsewhere

**Claim:** A preserved direction may recur elsewhere in the sixfold field.
