A direction that appears in one selected connection may appear again elsewhere in the sixfold field. The proof treats recurrence as evidence, not as assumption. A bearing preserved across distinct station connections can be recognized as the same direction recurring. What becomes available is direction recurrence inside the sixfold substrate. This prepares family language while keeping the source relation visible: the recurrence belongs to constructed station connections, not to an abstract plane.

---

### Theorem 323 — Same-Direction Connections Form Family

**Claim:** Connections with the same preserved direction form a direction family.

Connections that share a preserved direction can now gather into a family. The family is not imposed by notation; it is formed by repeated bearing among constructed station-to-station relations. The proof lets the common direction hold the connections together. What becomes available is direction-family structure: a way to organize same-bearing connections for later extension, transport, and coordinate audit. The family remains source-bound because only connections with the preserved direction may enter it.

---

### Theorem 324 — Three Direction Families Exposed

**Claim:** The sixfold boundary exposes three recurring direction families.

The sixfold boundary now exposes three recurring direction families. Selected station connections repeat their bearings in three native ways, and those repetitions organize the field. The proof does not borrow a grid or a theory of parallel classes. It reads what the closed six-station boundary and its connections make visible. What becomes available is the three-family direction scaffold, the native directional order from which later field extension and coordinate audit can begin.

---

### Theorem 325 — Direction-Family Evidence Available

**Claim:** Direction-family evidence is available in the constructed substrate.

The direction-family work closes by making its evidence available. The constructed substrate contains selected station connections, repeated bearings, and three direction families exposed by the sixfold boundary. This does not yet authorize every theorem about lines, parallels, or coordinates. It provides the evidence those later readings must spend. What stands is direction-family availability: the field can now carry, extend, and audit directions because their recurrence has been earned inside the construction itself.

---

### Theorem 326 — Repeated Triangular Field Availability

**Claim:** A repeated triangular field is available from extended direction families.

The extended direction families now yield more than isolated recurrence. Their crossings produce repeatable stations, their joins form local triangular cells, and shared sides let those cells stand beside one another. The proof therefore gathers the outward spread into a repeated triangular field. This is not a borrowed tessellation theorem and not a coordinate grid disguised as construction. It is the field produced when the three recurring direction families continue to meet under preserved relations. What becomes available is a repeated triangular substrate, broad enough for transport and audit while still answerable to the fixed-opening source that made the first cell lawful.

---

### Theorem 327 — Local Relation Inside One Cell

**Claim:** A local relation may appear inside one triangular cell.

The repeated field can now be inspected locally. Inside one triangular cell, stations, sides, and direction-family joins may supply a relation that was not being claimed for the whole field at once. The proof permits the relation to appear where its own cell has actually provided the necessary evidence. This is a narrowing move, not a generalization. The field is large, but the claim begins in one cell. What becomes available is local relation evidence: a relation may stand inside a triangular cell when that cell supplies the defining station and side conditions.

---

### Theorem 328 — Another Cell Preserves Same Structure

**Claim:** Another triangular cell may preserve the same relevant structure.

A second triangular cell may now be compared with the first, but only through structure. The proof does not say that the second cell is useful because it looks similar. It asks whether the relevant stations, sides, and direction-family roles are preserved there. When those conditions remain intact, the second cell can carry the same kind of local structure. What becomes available is a possible companion cell for inspection. What remains refused is visual analogy without preservation, because the proof field can reuse only what the construction has kept alive.

---

### Theorem 329 — Inspection in Other Cell

**Claim:** The preserved structure allows the local relation to be inspected in the other cell.

When the second cell preserves the relevant structure, the local relation from the first cell can be inspected there. The proof does not yet say the relation has been freely copied across the field. It says the other cell contains the conditions that made the relation meaningful, so the relation can be checked again under those conditions. This gives transport its first working motion in the repeated field: local evidence can be inspected elsewhere when the structure is preserved. The permission lands as inspection under preserved conditions, not as unbounded repetition.

---

### Theorem 330 — Reuse Requires Defining Conditions

**Claim:** The relation is reused only when its defining conditions are preserved.

The proof now states the rule that controls reuse. A relation does not travel because a later sentence wants it, and it does not travel because another picture resembles the first. It travels only when the defining conditions that made it true are preserved. Station roles, side witnesses, direction-family placement, or shared-cell structure must remain available. What becomes available is lawful reuse. What is refused is silent copying. The theorem gives the repeated field power without loosening the source boundary: reuse is allowed, but only with its receipt.

---

### Theorem 331 — Reuse Without New Opening

**Claim:** The reuse does not depend on a new compass opening.

Once the defining conditions are preserved, relation reuse does not require a new compass opening. The original fixed opening remains the root measure, and the repeated field carries its consequences through direction families and cell structure. The proof therefore avoids multiplying sources. A relation may reappear because its conditions survive, not because a fresh measuring act has been smuggled in. What becomes available is reuse under the old source authority. The fixed opening is not replaced; it is remembered through the relations it has already generated.

---

### Theorem 332 — Reuse Without Coordinate Generation

**Claim:** The reuse does not depend on coordinate generation.

Reuse also does not depend on coordinates creating the field. A coordinate record may later check a repeated station or compare a relation, but it is not the engine of reuse. The repeated triangular cell, the preserved station roles, and the direction-family structure do the constructional work. The proof again keeps audit below source. What becomes available is coordinate-free transport of preserved relations, with coordinates admitted only afterward as memory or verification. This protects the field from letting its bookkeeping pretend to be its birth.

---

### Theorem 333 — Transportability for Preserved Local Relations

**Claim:** Transportability is available for preserved local relations.

The previous refusals now gather into a positive permission. A local relation may travel through the repeated triangular field when the construction preserves the conditions that define it. It does not need a new compass opening, and it does not arise from coordinate generation. It needs the relevant station, side, direction, or cell relations to remain intact. What crystallises is transportability for preserved local relations. The field can now carry earned facts from one lawful cell to another, while resemblance, convenience, and analytic shortcuts stay outside the gate.

---

### Theorem 334 — Constructed Figure After Source Operations

**Claim:** A constructed figure is present only after source operations have produced stations, steps, cells, closure, direction, or transportable relations.

The book now tightens the word figure. A constructed figure is not whatever appears on the page. It is present only where source operations have produced the relations that make it count: stations, steps, cells, closure, direction families, or transportable relations. The proof refuses the temptation to treat sight as source. A visible form may invite attention, but constructional history gives it proof value. What becomes available is a disciplined figure standard. Later geometry may use figures, but only figures whose parts have been earned.

---

### Theorem 335 — Visual Feature Not Enough

**Claim:** A visual feature is not enough to count as proof.

A visual feature by itself cannot carry proof. A line may look straight, a pair may look equal, a crossing may look centered, but appearance does not preserve the source relation. The theorem refuses diagrammatic persuasion unless the feature is tied back to construction. This is not hostility to the visible figure; it is the condition under which the visible figure becomes trustworthy. What becomes available is a refusal-test for every later claim: if the feature is only seen and not earned, it cannot yet stand as proof.

---

### Theorem 336 — Claimed Relation Needs Source

**Claim:** A claimed relation must be tied to an earned source relation.

Every claimed relation now faces the same demand. It must be tied to an earned source relation before the proof can spend it. The tie may run through fixed opening, boundary generation, station selection, stepping, cell formation, closure, direction recurrence, transport, or audit after construction. Without such a tie, the claim is only a sentence. With it, the sentence can land. What becomes available is source-tethered relation language. What remains refused is any free relation asserted because it would be useful downstream.

---

### Theorem 337 — Equal-Side Claims Need Equal Opening

**Claim:** Equal-side claims must be tied to equal-opening evidence.

An equal-side claim may now be made only by returning to equal-opening evidence. The proof does not let equality enter as a general school category. A side is equal in this source-relevant sense when the same fixed opening witnesses the relevant reaches or steps. If that witness is missing, the equal-side claim has not been paid for. What becomes available is an equality filter for sides: equal-opening evidence licenses the claim; visual matching does not. Later cells and figures must answer to that evidence before using equal-side language.

---

### Theorem 338 — Boundary Claims Need Fixed Sweep

**Claim:** Boundary claims must be tied to fixed-sweep generation.

Boundary language must remember its source. A boundary counts here because a fixed compass opening was swept from a selected center, leaving reached positions under one center-bound relation. A later claim about a boundary must therefore point back to fixed-sweep generation or to a transported condition that lawfully preserves it. The proof refuses generic curve language. What becomes available is source-bound boundary use: boundary claims may stand where the fixed sweep or its earned local analogue supplies the relation.

---

### Theorem 339 — Station Claims Need Selection

**Claim:** Station claims must be tied to selected station availability.

Stationhood is now placed under its own discipline. A point is not a station merely because it is visible. It becomes a station when the construction selects it, generates it by direction-family crossing, or preserves a role that allows it to support later action. The proof therefore ties station claims to selected station availability. What becomes available is controlled station language throughout the growing field. What remains refused is any claim that a visible mark can act without having first received station authority.

---

### Theorem 340 — Step Claims Need Fixed Opening

**Claim:** Step claims must be tied to fixed-opening boundary stepping.

A step claim must be tied to the fixed-opening step that earned stepping in the first place. Adjacent boundary stations were joined by one preserved opening, and repeated local steps carry authority only through that relation or a preserved transport of it. The proof refuses to call any short segment a step just because it looks like one. What becomes available is step language under measure witness. A step may be spent when fixed-opening evidence supplies it; otherwise the word remains unavailable.

---

### Theorem 341 — Cell Claims Need Station Pairs

**Claim:** Cell claims must be tied to supplied station-pair relations.

A cell claim must show its station-pair relations. Three stations do not become a triangular cell just by sitting near one another. The joins among them must be supplied, and the side relations that bound the cell must be available. The proof therefore returns every cell claim to its pairwise evidence. What becomes available is cellhood with a receipt: a cell stands when its station pairs are lawfully held. This protects later assemblies from treating any visible cluster as proof-bearing.

---

### Theorem 342 — Closure Claims Need Six-Step Return

**Claim:** Closure claims must be tied to six-step return evidence.

Closure is not a feeling of completeness. In this source field, first closure was earned by six equal-opening boundary steps returning to the starting station. Any later claim of closure must therefore identify the return or the preserved structure that supplies it. The proof refuses to confuse a finished-looking drawing with a closed construction. What becomes available is return-based closure language. Closure may be spent where the source walk has come back to its beginning; otherwise the demand remains open.

---

### Theorem 343 — Transported Claims Need Preserved Conditions

**Claim:** Transported claims must be tied to preservation of defining conditions.

A transported claim must bring its conditions with it. The proof does not allow a result to leap from one cell to another without the station roles, side relations, direction-family structure, or audit conditions that defined it. Transport is therefore a preservation event, not a copying event. What becomes available is a rule for later movement through the field: carried claims must carry their defining conditions. Anything less is resemblance, and resemblance is not proof.

---

### Theorem 344 — Proof-Bearing Figure

**Claim:** A figure becomes proof-bearing only when construction has forced the relation it claims.

The figure can now be trusted only where construction has forced the relation it claims. A drawn form may guide attention, but it becomes proof-bearing when the underlying operations have made its stations, sides, closure, direction, or transport relations stand. This theorem gathers the previous filters into one standard. What becomes available is proof-bearing figurehood: the visible form may participate in proof when its claim is backed by source operations. What remains refused is the old habit of letting a diagram prove by looking right.

### Theorem 345 — Sixfold Boundary Closure Earned

**Claim:** Sixfold boundary closure has been earned.

The proof now returns to the sixfold source and names what has been earned. Boundary stepping under the fixed opening has produced a six-step return to the starting station. What stands is sixfold boundary closure: a completed source circuit that can support central cell count, half-turn reading, and later connector work without importing polygon theory.

**Claim:** Each adjacent pair forms a central cell with the center.

The result still refuses full triangle theory, but it has enough source evidence to assemble the six-cell turn.

### Theorem 348 — Six Cells Meet Around Center

**Claim:** The six cells meet around the center station.

The six central cells now share a common center. Each cell uses the center and one adjacent boundary pair, so the cells occur in order around that source station. What becomes available is around-center meeting of the six cells, the immediate shape of the turn field before any degree language has appeared.

The final central cell is controlled by the sixth boundary step. Because the boundary walk returns to the starting station, the last adjacent pair closes the cell sequence back to the beginning. What becomes available is a central cell circuit whose last member returns to the start, giving the turn field a source-earned end.

**Claim:** No gap remains around the center after the sixth cell.

After the sixth cell returns to the starting boundary station, the proof asks whether any central gap remains. Within the constructed circuit, none does. The final movement is that notation alone cannot justify the equality. What becomes available is later algebra may remain source-bound. ## Appendices and Glossary

The appendices appear here in the shell so the book has its reference apparatus from the beginning. Later drafts can move these to the back, but keeping them visible during composition protects the paragraph writing from drift.

## Appendix A  -  Canonical Paragraph Stencil

Each readable block paragraph should silently preserve this structure:

[Block ID]  -  [Title]. The claim asks for [target permission] to stand. The proof begins by [first movement], then [middle movement], then [refusal, preservation, expansion, integration, or closure]. As the sentences contract, [dominant operator pattern] does the main work: [brief axes movement]. The block lands by making [new affordance] available while refusing [unearned import]. From here, later proof may spend [permission] but may not yet spend [forbidden stronger claim].

The paragraph should not become a miniature essay. It should make the block readable while preserving proof pressure.

## Appendix B  -  Sentence-Walk Receipt

**1. Sentence**Quote exactly. Do not paraphrase before classification.

**2. Axes**Operator Â· Orientation Â· Remainder.

**3. Contraction name**Fixed name from the 54-cell table. Do not localize, rename, or improve it case by case.

**4. Ultra-short form**Fixed X-Y form from the lookup table.

**5. Crystallisation**One compact sentence showing how this exact sentence contracts into the fixed cell: Operator does the work; Orientation gives the direction of resolution; Remainder names what is left standing.

**6. Landing**One sentence saying what is now allowed, refused, preserved, completed, or made stand.

## Appendix C  -  Axis Glossary

### Operators

**Distinction** places, bounds, selects, excludes, narrows, assigns, or fixes something as this rather than that.

**Expansion** opens reach, field, spread, multiplicity, continuation, or outward availability.

**Relation** maintains bearing between terms, roles, positions, conditions, or patterns.

**Correction** revises, redirects, repairs, reweights, cancels, or replaces a live prior reading.

**Integration** organizes parts into one functional arrangement, system, body, field, or unit.

**Closure** satisfies a live local demand, validates, preserves, exhausts, or brings continuation to rest.

### Orientations

**Toward** is focal convergence into a target, formed unit, role, product, placement, or state.

**Across** is retained span across terms, levels, comparisons, dependencies, routes, or repeated cases.

**Away** is release from a prior frame, false source, dependency, threat, confinement, or rejected path.

### Remainders

**Is** leaves a standing condition, status, identity, role, fact, availability, or fixedness.

**With** leaves held bearing: dependency, pairing, correspondence, balance, support, shared pattern, or mutual reference.

**Does** leaves an enacted outcome: operation, production, transformation, field generation, structure formation, completion, or event.

## Appendix F  -  Editorial Rules for Appending

**One block, one paragraph.** The paragraph may mention the claim, the proof movement, the dominant contractions, the crystallised permission, and the refusal. It should not become a full table.

**No local renaming.** If contraction names are mentioned, use the fixed names from Appendix D.

**No unearned branch language.** Geometry, trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and algebra appear only when their source permissions have been made active.

**End in permission or refusal.** Each paragraph should leave the reader knowing what the block now allows and what it still does not allow.

The First Fixed Opening Â· Canonical HTML source book Â· 615 readable block paragraphs

# Appendix B - Editorial Source Notes

This appendix is reserved for editorial notes that help maintain the book.

It should not interrupt the learner-facing path.

The main chapters should stay visual and construction-first. Appendix A holds the full source ledger. Appendix C and Appendix D track dependencies and chapter references.
# Appendix C - Dependency Index

This appendix tracks the permissions the main text depends on.

The main chapters should stay light and visual. They should not carry the full proof ledger. But every learner-facing claim still needs a place in the source structure.

Use this index as a writing and audit tool.

A permission is ready for the main text when the reader can answer three questions:

1. Where can I first see it?
2. What action made it available?
3. What later idea depends on it?

The index below follows the book's main spine.

---

## Fixed Opening

**First appears:** Chapter 1.

**Visible source:** The compass is opened once. The distance between compass point and pencil point is kept unchanged.

**Permission:** The same compass opening can be used again.

**Feeds:** Boundary, station transfer, equal-opening step, first triangle, sixfold closure.

**Main-text wording:** The opening stays the same.

**Do not say too early:** Invariant under transformation.

**Source reference:** Affordance 001; Theorem 001.

---

## Boundary

**First appears:** Chapter 2.

**Visible source:** The compass point stays at the center while the pencil sweeps with the fixed opening.

**Permission:** A fixed opening swept from a center makes a boundary.

**Feeds:** Boundary stations, boundary steps, first triangle, closure.

**Main-text wording:** A point stays still. The pencil moves around it. The reach stays the same. A boundary appears.

**Do not say too early:** Circle theorem, locus theorem, equation of a circle.

**Source reference:** Affordance 002; Theorem 002.

---

## Station

**First appears:** Chapter 3.

**Visible source:** A point on the boundary is selected. The compass point is allowed to stand there.

**Permission:** A selected boundary point can become a compass station.

**Feeds:** Station sweep, equal-opening step, step sequence, field extension.

**Main-text wording:** We choose a point on the boundary. Now the compass can stand there.

**Do not say too early:** Arbitrary point selection over a continuum, general point-set language.

**Source reference:** Affordance 003; Theorem 003.

---

## Step

**First appears:** Chapter 4.

**Visible source:** The compass stands at a boundary station and sweeps with the same opening. The new sweep meets the original boundary.

**Permission:** One boundary station can step to another boundary station by the fixed opening.

**Feeds:** First triangle, six-step closure, hexagon, direction pairs.

**Main-text wording:** A reaches B by the same opening.

**Do not say too early:** Chord theory, central angle measure, polygon side theorem.

**Source reference:** Affordance 004; Theorems 004, 014-016.

---

## Cell

**First appears:** Chapter 5.

**Visible source:** The center and two adjacent boundary stations form a triangle. Each side is witnessed by the same opening.

**Permission:** The center and two adjacent boundary stations form the first equal-opening triangular cell.

**Feeds:** Six central cells, triangular field, rhombus as two cells, area by cell count.

**Main-text wording:** Three stations. Three sides. One opening.

**Do not say too early:** Equilateral triangle theorem, 60 degree angles, trigonometric values.

**Source reference:** Affordance 005; Theorems 005, 018-020.

---

## Closure

**First appears:** Chapter 6.

**Visible source:** The equal-opening step repeats around the boundary and returns to the starting station on the sixth step.

**Permission:** The boundary cycle closes after six equal-opening steps.

**Feeds:** Six central cells, hexagon, direction families, turn field.

**Main-text wording:** The sixth step returns to the start.

**Do not say too early:** Rotational symmetry as a starting theorem, 360 degrees, regular polygon theory.

**Source reference:** Affordance 006; Theorems 006, 017, 023-024.

---

## Turn

**First appears:** Chapter 7.

**Visible source:** Six equal-opening triangular cells meet around the center.

**Permission:** Six equal-opening cells fill the turn around the center.

**Feeds:** Hexagon as six cells, direction pairs, field extension, figure reading.

**Main-text wording:** Six cells meet around the center and fill one complete turn.

**Do not say too early:** Angle sum theorem, degree division, group symmetry.

**Source reference:** Affordance 007; Theorems 007, 021-032.

---

## Direction Family

**First appears:** Chapter 8 and Chapter 9.

**Visible source:** Opposite sides of the hexagon run the same way. The six sides fall into three same-way pairs.

**Permission:** Recurring same-way lines may be gathered into direction families.

**Feeds:** Field extension, triangular tessellation, lines, slope, coordinate records.

**Main-text wording:** Look at the hexagon. Its sides come in three pairs. Each pair goes the same way.

**Do not say too early:** Slope, Cartesian axes, general parallel-line theory.

**Source reference:** Affordance 008; Theorems 033-034; Theorems 086-089; Theorem 255.

---

## Tessellation

**First appears:** Chapter 10.

**Visible source:** The three direction families continue. Where they meet, new stations and repeated triangular cells appear.

**Permission:** The sixfold construction can extend into a triangular field.

**Feeds:** Transport, geometry, adjacency, area, rightness, coordinate audit.

**Main-text wording:** The first construction has not been replaced. It has been extended.

**Do not say too early:** Arbitrary tiling theory, square grid, coordinate plane as source.

**Source reference:** Affordance 011; Theorems 035-037, 053-057.

---

## Transport

**First appears:** Chapter 11.

**Visible source:** A relation in one cell is compared with another cell that carries the same supporting structure.

**Permission:** Local relations can travel across the field when their source conditions travel with them.

**Feeds:** Geometry, area, rightness, coordinate audit, symbolic memory.

**Main-text wording:** The receipt must travel with the claim.

**Do not say too early:** Abstract isomorphism, invariance under station transfer, proof-ledger machinery.

**Source reference:** Affordance 012; Theorems 038-042.

---

## Figure Reading

**First appears:** Chapter 12.

**Visible source:** A triangle, a rhombus, and a hexagon are read from cells the field already made.

**Permission:** Geometry may read constructed figures as source-earned bodies.

**Feeds:** Sides, adjacency, paths, area, right triangles.

**Main-text wording:** The field comes first. The figure comes after.

**Do not say too early:** Full imported theorem lists for triangles, rhombi, or hexagons.

**Source reference:** Bridge Blocks 256-271.

---

## Adjacency and Path

**First appears:** Chapter 13.

**Visible source:** Two triangular cells share a side. A chain of such cells can be followed through shared sides.

**Permission:** The triangular field supports sides, shared sides, adjacent cells, and paths.

**Feeds:** Area decomposition, transport, geometry examples.

**Main-text wording:** A side can be an edge. A shared side can be a passage.

**Do not say too early:** Graph theory, shortest path theory, coordinate distance.

**Source reference:** Theorems 047-052.

---

## Area by Cell Count

**First appears:** Chapter 14.

**Visible source:** One triangular cell is counted as one native area seed. Larger regions are counted by lawful cell decomposition.

**Permission:** Area may be read by lawful cell count before formula.

**Feeds:** Rhombus area, hexagon area, later area formulas.

**Main-text wording:** Area begins as counted structure.

**Do not say too early:** Base times height, square units as first authority, arbitrary curved area.

**Source reference:** Theorems 090, 252-255.

---

## Rightness

**First appears:** Chapter 15.

**Visible source:** Selected connectors inside the field meet cleanly. The right mark is added only after the supporting connectors are visible.

**Permission:** Selected connectors inside the constructed field may meet right.

**Feeds:** Field-right triangle, ratio packet, trigonometry.

**Main-text wording:** One connector goes this way. Another meets it without leaning with it.

**Do not say too early:** Slope perpendicularity, coordinate perpendicularity, general right-angle theory.

**Source reference:** Affordance 010; Affordance 010; Theorem 010.

---

## Field-Right Triangle

**First appears:** Chapter 16.

**Visible source:** A right meeting and two selected field stations form a right triangle inside the field.

**Permission:** A field-right triangle can be read from a right meeting inside the constructed field.

**Feeds:** Ratio packet, trigonometric side roles, unit-circle organization.

**Main-text wording:** The triangle is not an isolated drawing. It is a piece of the construction.

**Do not say too early:** Sine, cosine, tangent, detached right-triangle exercises.

**Source reference:** Theorems 395-398; Theorem 417.

---

## Ratio

**First appears:** Chapter 17.

**Visible source:** The sides of the field-right triangle are compared from a chosen acute corner.

**Permission:** Ratio is available as an angle-bound comparison inside a field-right triangle.

**Feeds:** Sine, cosine, tangent, trigonometric relations.

**Main-text wording:** The ratios come first. The names come later.

**Do not say too early:** Trig tables, unit circle as first source, function notation as primitive.

**Source reference:** Theorem 417 and related ratio-packet material.

---

## Trigonometric Names

**First appears:** Chapter 18.

**Visible source:** Opposite, adjacent, and hypotenuse are read relative to one chosen acute angle.

**Permission:** Sine, cosine, and tangent may be used as names for field-earned side ratios.

**Feeds:** Trigonometric relations, unit-circle organization, coordinate checks.

**Main-text wording:** The names record ratios that have already appeared.

**Do not say too early:** Detached identity manipulation, memorized ratios without a triangle.

**Source reference:** Theorems 411-418.

---

## Trigonometric Relations

**First appears:** Chapter 19.

**Visible source:** Sine, cosine, and tangent reuse the same three sides of one right triangle.

**Permission:** Basic trigonometric relations may be treated as compressed memory of one field-earned right triangle.

**Feeds:** Unit-circle organization, algebraic transformation of trig records.

**Main-text wording:** The three names come from one triangle. That is why they know each other.

**Do not say too early:** Symbol-only proof of identities.

**Source reference:** Theorems 409-418; Theorems 419 onward.

---

## Unit-Circle Organization

**First appears:** Chapter 20.

**Visible source:** A radius of length 1 forms a right triangle by projection.

**Permission:** The unit circle may organize already-earned trigonometric ratios.

**Feeds:** Coordinate trigonometry, later symbolic records.

**Main-text wording:** The circle did not invent the ratios. The circle gives them a clean place to live.

**Do not say too early:** Unit circle as first source of trigonometry.

**Source reference:** Theorems 414-415 and related unit-circle material.

---

## Coordinate Audit

**First appears:** Chapter 21.

**Visible source:** Coordinate labels are added to stations after the field already exists.

**Permission:** Coordinate labels may record and check constructed positions.

**Feeds:** Differences, distance checks, slope, line equations, algebraic records.

**Main-text wording:** Coordinates come after the picture. They help us check what is already there.

**Do not say too early:** Coordinates generating the field, square grid as substrate.

**Source reference:** Affordance 009; Theorems 009 and 480.

---

## Difference and Norm

**First appears:** Chapter 22.

**Visible source:** Two coordinate records are compared. Their difference records a move, and a norm checks distance.

**Permission:** Coordinate differences can record movement, and distance rules can audit constructed distance.

**Feeds:** Coordinate geometry, slope, line equations, algebraic distance relations.

**Main-text wording:** The picture builds. The coordinate record checks.

**Do not say too early:** Calculation replacing constructional evidence.

**Source reference:** Theorems 154-159; Theorem 480.

---

## Analytic Direction

**First appears:** Chapter 23.

**Visible source:** A visible direction family receives a coordinate record such as slope or a line equation.

**Permission:** Analytic line language may record native direction-family structure.

**Feeds:** Coordinate geometry, algebraic line equations, symbolic transformation.

**Main-text wording:** The line in the field comes first. The slope is a later record.

**Do not say too early:** Slope as the source of direction, equations creating lines.

**Source reference:** Theorems 033-037; Theorems 154-159; Theorem 480.

---

## Symbolic Memory

**First appears:** Chapter 24.

**Visible source:** A visible relation in a figure or field is named with a symbol.

**Permission:** Symbols may carry source-earned relation memory.

**Feeds:** Equations, unknowns, formulas, algebraic transformation.

**Main-text wording:** Algebra remembers relations. It does not invent them.

**Do not say too early:** Free symbol manipulation detached from source.

**Source reference:** Theorems 154-155; Theorems 584-587.

---

## Equation and Unknown

**First appears:** Chapter 25.

**Visible source:** A missing part of a visible relation is marked with a symbol and constrained by an equation.

**Permission:** Equations can constrain and recover unknown relations.

**Feeds:** Solving, transformation, formulas.

**Main-text wording:** Solving is relation recovery.

**Do not say too early:** Guessing, symbol shuffling without relation preservation.

**Source reference:** Theorems 584-585.

---

## Algebraic Transformation

**First appears:** Chapter 26.

**Visible source:** A source-earned relation is recorded symbolically and changed form by equality-preserving moves.

**Permission:** Algebra is source-bound symbolic transformation.

**Feeds:** Final branch convergence and later advanced algebra.

**Main-text wording:** The form can change. The relation must stay true.

**Do not say too early:** Algebra replacing the construction as authority.

**Source reference:** Theorems 584, 586, 587.

---

## Dependency Chain Summary

Fixed opening feeds boundary.

Boundary feeds station.

Station feeds step.

Step feeds cell.

Cell feeds closure.

Closure feeds turn.

Turn feeds hexagon.

Hexagon feeds direction family.

Direction family feeds tessellation.

Tessellation feeds transport.

Transport feeds source-earned geometry.

Geometry feeds adjacency, area, and rightness.

Rightness feeds the field-right triangle.

The field-right triangle feeds ratio.

Ratio feeds trigonometric names.

Trigonometric names feed trigonometric relations and unit-circle organization.

The constructed field feeds coordinate audit.

Coordinate audit feeds difference, norm, slope, and line records.

All visible relations feed symbolic memory.

Symbolic memory feeds equations.

Equations feed lawful transformation.

The first opening stays.

At the end, truth stays.

---
# Appendix D - Theorem-to-Chapter Crosswalk

This appendix links each learner-facing chapter to its source support.

The main chapters should not become proof ledgers. They should stay visual, plain, and readable.

But the writer still needs to know what each chapter is allowed to use.

Use this crosswalk as an audit sheet.

For each chapter, track:

- the permission the chapter earns
- the source blocks or source region behind it
- what the main text may say
- what the main text should not import early
- any audit note that belongs outside the learner-facing prose

---

| Chapter | Permission Earned | Source Blocks | Main Text May Say | Do Not Import Early | Audit Note |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Chapter 1 - The Fixed Opening | The same compass opening can be used again after the compass point moves. | Affordance 001; Theorem 001 | The opening stays the same. The compass can move without forgetting its opening. | Boundary, triangle, coordinate, invariant language. | This is the first source invariant, but do not use the word invariant in the main chapter. |
| Chapter 2 - A Sweep Becomes a Boundary | A fixed opening swept from a center makes a boundary. | Affordance 002; Theorem 002 | The point stays. The pencil moves. The reach stays the same. A boundary appears. | Circle theorems, locus theorem, equation of a circle. | Circle vocabulary may appear later, after boundary is visually secure. |
| Chapter 3 - Boundary Positions Become Stations | A selected point on the boundary can become a new compass station. | Affordance 003; Theorem 003 | We choose a point on the boundary. Now the compass can stand there. | General point-set language, every boundary point as already active. | Keep selection modest: one chosen position becomes usable. |
| Chapter 4 - The First Boundary Step | One boundary station can step to another boundary station by the fixed opening. | Affordance 004; Theorems 004, 014-016 | A reaches B by the same opening. | Chord theory, hexagon closure, central angles. | This chapter earns only one step, not the whole cycle. |
| Chapter 5 - The First Triangle | The center and two adjacent boundary stations form the first equal-opening triangle. | Affordance 005; Theorems 005, 018-020 | Three stations. Three sides. One opening. | Angle measure, 60 degrees, equilateral theorem as outside import. | The word triangle is safe after the three stations and three sides are visible. |
| Chapter 6 - Six Steps Close | The boundary cycle closes after six equal-opening steps. | Affordance 006; Theorems 006, 017, 023-024 | The sixth step returns to the start. | Rotational symmetry as source, 360 degrees, regular polygon theory. | Closure should be shown as return before being named. |
| Chapter 7 - Six Triangles Around a Center | Six equal-opening cells fill the turn around the center. | Affordance 007; Theorems 007, 021-032 | Six cells meet around the center. Together they fill the turn. | Degree division, angle-sum proof, group language. | This is the first central turn field. |
| Chapter 8 - The Hexagon Inside the Construction | The hexagon can be read as a six-cell source-earned body; opposite side pairs prepare direction. | Theorems 086-089; Theorems 252-255; direction-family preparation | The hexagon is the outside of the six cells. Its sides come in three same-way pairs. | Regular polygon theorem list, slope, coordinate axes. | This chapter may introduce direction visually before Chapter 9 develops direction families. |
| Chapter 9 - Direction Appears | Recurring same-way lines may be gathered into direction families. | Affordance 008; Theorems 033-034 | Some sides go the same way. Those are our first direction families. | Slope, Cartesian axes, full parallel-line theory. | Direction must stay visible and pre-coordinate. |
| Chapter 10 - Extending the Field | The sixfold construction can extend into a triangular field. | Affordance 011; Theorems 035-037, 053-057 | The directions continue. Where they meet, new stations and cells appear. | Arbitrary tilings, square grid, coordinate plane as source. | The field grows from the construction; it is not a background grid. |
| Chapter 11 - Transport Without Cheating | Local relations can travel across the field only when their source conditions travel with them. | Affordance 012; Theorems 038-042 | The receipt must travel with the claim. | Appearance-only copying, abstract isomorphism, proof-ledger machinery. | This chapter protects later reuse across the field. |
| Chapter 12 - Figures as Source-Earned Bodies | Geometry may read constructed figures as source-earned bodies. | Bridge Blocks 256-271 | The field comes first. The figure comes after. | Imported theorem lists for named figures. | Geometry begins as reading, not as outside classification. |
| Chapter 13 - Sides, Adjacency, and Paths | The triangular field supports sides, shared sides, adjacent cells, and paths. | Theorems 047-052 | A side can be an edge. A shared side can be a passage. | Graph theory, shortest-path theory, coordinate distance. | This chapter gives movement language through the field. |
| Chapter 14 - Area Begins with Cells | Area may be read by lawful cell count before formula. | Theorems 090, 252-255 | Area begins as counted structure. | Base times height, square units as first authority, arbitrary curved regions. | Area formulas can come later as memory of counted or decomposed regions. |
| Chapter 15 - Right Meetings in the Field | Selected connectors inside the constructed field may meet right. | Affordance 010; Affordance 010; Theorem 010 | Some selected connectors meet cleanly. The right mark comes after the meeting is earned. | Slope perpendicularity, coordinate perpendicularity, every square-looking corner marked right. | This is a bridge chapter. Keep it careful and visual. |
| Chapter 16 - The First Field-Right Triangle | A field-right triangle can be read from a right meeting inside the constructed field. | Theorems 395-398; Theorem 417 | The triangle is a piece of the construction. | Sine, cosine, tangent, detached right-triangle diagrams. | The field should remain visible behind the triangle. |
| Chapter 17 - The First Ratio Packet | Ratio is available as an angle-bound comparison inside a field-right triangle. | Theorem 417 and related ratio-packet material | The ratios come first. The names come later. | Trig names as source, unit circle as source, trig tables. | The 1, sqrt(3), 2 packet needs a clear bridge from field-rightness. |
| Chapter 18 - Sine, Cosine, Tangent | Sine, cosine, and tangent may be used as names for field-earned side ratios. | Theorems 411-418 | The names record ratios already visible in the triangle. | Detached functions, identity manipulation, memorized table first. | Keep the chosen angle visible. |
| Chapter 19 - Trigonometric Relations | Basic trigonometric relations may be treated as compressed memory of one field-earned right triangle. | Theorems 409-418; Theorems 419 onward | The three names come from one triangle. That is why they know each other. | Symbol-only identity proof. | Relation language should remain tied to the side packet. |
| Chapter 20 - The Unit Circle Comes Later | The unit circle may organize already-earned trigonometric ratios. | Theorems 414-415 and related unit-circle material | The circle did not invent the ratios. It gives them a clean place to live. | Unit circle as first source of trigonometry. | The chapter must explicitly preserve the prior triangle source. |
| Chapter 21 - Coordinates as Records | Coordinate labels may record and check constructed positions. | Affordance 009; Theorems 009, 480 | Coordinates come after the picture. They help us check what is already there. | Coordinates generating the field, square grid as substrate. | If using axial coordinates, disclose orientation and basis convention in notes. |
| Chapter 22 - Differences and Distance Checks | Coordinate differences can record movement, and distance rules can audit constructed distance. | Theorems 154-159; Theorem 480 | The picture builds. The coordinate record checks. | Coordinate calculation replacing constructional evidence. | This is audit language, not source language. |
| Chapter 23 - Lines, Slopes, and Direction Families | Analytic line language may record native direction-family structure. | Theorems 033-037; Theorems 154-159; Theorem 480 | The line in the field comes first. Slope is a later record. | Slope as source of direction, equations creating lines. | The direction-family picture should remain available. |
| Chapter 24 - Symbols Remember Relations | Symbols may carry source-earned relation memory. | Theorems 154-155; Theorems 584-587 | Algebra remembers relations. It does not invent them. | Free symbol manipulation detached from source. | This begins algebra as memory, not as machinery. |
| Chapter 25 - Equations and Unknowns | Equations can constrain and recover unknown relations. | Theorems 584-585 | Solving is relation recovery. | Guessing, rule-only symbol shuffling. | Unknowns must be attached to visible relations. |
| Chapter 26 - Lawful Transformation | Algebra is source-bound symbolic transformation. | Theorems 584, 586, 587 | The form can change. The relation must stay true. | Algebra replacing constructional authority. | This is the final permission of the main book. |

---

## Crosswalk Notes for Revision

1. The main chapters should cite source references briefly, but should not explain the full proof ledger.
2. If a chapter begins to sound like an audit note, move that language into Appendix A, B, C, or D.
3. Chapters 15-20 require the most careful bridge work because rightness, ratio, and trigonometry must remain field-earned.
4. Chapters 21-23 require careful coordinate language. Coordinates are records and checks, not constructional sources.
5. Chapters 24-26 require careful algebra language. Symbols remember relations; they do not invent them.
6. Diagram captions should use the same permissions listed here.
7. Appendix F should remain the minimum visual contract for the first runtime or illustration pass.
8. When the 615-block source ledger is integrated into Appendix A, update the Source Blocks column with exact block identifiers.

---

## Chapter Boundary Audit

Use this quick checklist during final chapter revision.

| Chapter Range | Main Risk | Revision Check |
|---|---|---|
| Chapters 1-3 | Naming too much too soon | Does the reader see opening, boundary, and station before formal names carry weight? |
| Chapters 4-8 | Turning closure into polygon theory too quickly | Does the construction step, close, and form cells before the hexagon is named? |
| Chapters 9-11 | Making direction too abstract | Does direction remain same-way visual recurrence before coordinate language? |
| Chapters 12-14 | Importing geometry formulas early | Are figures and area read from cells before formulas appear? |
| Chapters 15-17 | Assuming rightness and ratio | Are right meetings and side packets visibly earned? |
| Chapters 18-20 | Making trigonometry name-first | Do sine, cosine, tangent, and the unit circle come after the ratios? |
| Chapters 21-23 | Letting coordinates create the field | Are coordinates consistently described as records and audits? |
| Chapters 24-26 | Letting algebra detach | Does each symbol or transformation remember a source-earned relation? |

---
# Appendix E - Diagram Inventory

This appendix tracks the full diagram plan for the book.

Appendix F gives the first 24 diagrams to create. Appendix E keeps the larger inventory and records what each diagram is supposed to do.

A diagram in this book should not merely decorate a page. It should carry one piece of mathematical memory.

For each diagram, the useful briefing fields are:

- **Status:** current production state.
- **Purpose:** why the diagram exists.
- **Must show:** the visual facts the reader needs.
- **Must not show yet:** material that would rush the sequence.
- **Reader takeaway:** the sentence the picture should make easy to say.
- **Role:** how the diagram serves the book.

When a later production pass creates finished art or runtime views, add file names, export notes, and accessibility text to the Notes field.

---

---

## E.3 Production Notes

1. Produce Appendix F diagrams first.
2. Keep early diagrams visually sparse.
3. Use the same visual object again when it carries memory.
4. Do not use coordinates before the field exists.
5. Do not use trigonometric names before ratios are visible.
6. Do not use algebraic transformations before relation memory is established.
7. Add right marks only after the supporting connectors are shown.
8. Treat colour as helpful, not required. Use line style, marks, spacing, and labels so diagrams still work without colour.
9. Add alt text for every final diagram.
10. When using the Theorem Zero runtime, record which diagrams are source-native, overlay-derived, reader maps, or symbolic-memory diagrams.

---
# Appendix F - Minimum Viable Diagram Set

This appendix defines the first 24 diagrams to create.

These diagrams are not decoration. They are the visual spine of the book. Most of them are selected views of the same fixed-opening construction. A few are reader maps or symbolic records of what the construction has already earned.

For each diagram, the brief identifies:

- placement
- purpose
- must show
- must not show yet
- reader takeaway
- role in the book

The diagram rule stays the same:

Look first. Name later.

The picture should carry the memory. The words should only point.

---

## Diagram F.1 - Whole Derivation Map

**Placement:** Front matter, near Diagram 0.1.

**Purpose:** Show the whole path of the book in one visible chain.

**Must show:** Fixed opening to boundary to stations to equal-opening step to equal-opening cell to sixfold closure to direction families to triangular field to geometry, rightness, ratio, trigonometry, coordinate audit, symbolic memory, and algebraic transformation.

**Must not show yet:** Proof machinery, theorem numbers, coordinate formulas, or source-ledger machinery.

**Reader takeaway:** The book is one growth path, not a pile of separate topics.

**Role:** Reader map. It is not a construction diagram. It helps the reader know where the journey is going.

**Runtime note:** This can be a static map outside the Theorem Zero runtime, or a very light opening overlay that points from source action to later branches.

---

## Diagram F.2 - Branch Map

**Placement:** Front matter, near Diagram 0.2.

**Purpose:** Show that geometry, trigonometry, coordinate geometry, and algebra are branches from the same constructed field.

**Must show:** One central field or source trunk branching into the four later subjects.

**Must not show yet:** Detailed formulas, trig tables, coordinate grids as source, or algebraic manipulation rules.

**Reader takeaway:** The later subjects are not strangers. They grow from the same construction.

**Role:** Reader map and promise keeper.

**Runtime note:** This can remain a simple static diagram. It does not need to be generated from the substrate.

---

## Diagram F.3 - Fixed Compass Opening

**Placement:** Chapter 1.

**Purpose:** Show the first object of attention: the unchanged compass opening.

**Must show:** Compass point, pencil point, and the fixed opening between them.

**Must not show yet:** Boundary, circle, triangle, stations, or coordinates.

**Reader takeaway:** The opening is the first thing that stays the same.

**Role:** Source-native beginning. It establishes the first invariant.

**Runtime note:** This may be drawn outside the substrate as the pre-field action.

---

## Diagram F.4 - Same Opening After Transfer

**Placement:** Chapter 1.

**Purpose:** Show that the same opening can be used again after the compass moves.

**Must show:** The compass opening at one station and the same opening at another station, with matching opening marks.

**Must not show yet:** Boundary generation, triangle construction, or step closure.

**Reader takeaway:** The compass can move without forgetting its opening.

**Role:** First reuse permission.

**Runtime note:** This can be shown as a simple transfer animation or static two-position comparison.

---

## Diagram F.5 - Center Sweep Boundary

**Placement:** Chapter 2.

**Purpose:** Show a fixed opening sweeping around a center to make the first boundary.

**Must show:** Center point, fixed reach, sweep trace, and reached boundary.

**Must not show yet:** General circle theorems, chords, tangent lines, degrees, or equations.

**Reader takeaway:** A boundary appears because the reach from the center stays the same.

**Role:** Turns fixed opening into the first generated object.

**Runtime note:** This aligns with the circle-sweep overlay in the Theorem Zero runtime.

---

## Diagram F.6 - Boundary Stations

**Placement:** Chapter 3.

**Purpose:** Show that selected boundary positions can become compass stations.

**Must show:** Center, boundary, one or more selected boundary points, and the idea that the compass can stand on a chosen point.

**Must not show yet:** Every possible boundary point in use, six-step closure, or full field extension.

**Reader takeaway:** The boundary gives places where the compass can stand.

**Role:** Station permission.

**Runtime note:** This aligns with first-circle generated perimeter stations.

---

## Diagram F.7 - Boundary Station Sweep

**Placement:** Chapter 4.

**Purpose:** Show the compass standing at a boundary station and sweeping with the same opening.

**Must show:** Original center, original boundary, chosen boundary station, and a second sweep centered at that station.

**Must not show yet:** Full hexagon, six cells, direction families, or tessellation.

**Reader takeaway:** The same opening can now work from a boundary station.

**Role:** Prepares the first equal-opening boundary step.

**Runtime note:** This is an overlay-derived view of a station-centered sweep.

---

## Diagram F.8 - Equal-Opening Step

**Placement:** Chapter 4.

**Purpose:** Show the first step from one boundary station to the next.

**Must show:** Two adjacent boundary stations joined by a segment marked with the same opening used from the center.

**Must not show yet:** Six-step closure or polygon vocabulary.

**Reader takeaway:** One boundary station can reach another by the fixed opening.

**Role:** Step permission.

**Runtime note:** This is native to the unit-neighbour relation.

---

## Diagram F.9 - First Equal-Opening Triangle

**Placement:** Chapter 5.

**Purpose:** Show the first triangular cell made from the center and two adjacent boundary stations.

**Must show:** O, A, B, with OA, OB, and AB marked as the same opening.

**Must not show yet:** Angle measure, 60 degrees, trigonometry, or the word equilateral before the picture is clear.

**Reader takeaway:** Three stations. Three sides. One opening.

**Role:** First cell permission.

**Runtime note:** This is source-native: one center plus two adjacent unit neighbours.

---

## Diagram F.10 - Six-Step Boundary Closure

**Placement:** Chapter 6.

**Purpose:** Show the equal-opening step repeated around the boundary until it returns to the start.

**Must show:** Six boundary stations, six equal steps, and the final return to the first station.

**Must not show yet:** Rotational symmetry as a starting theorem, degree measure, or general polygon theory.

**Reader takeaway:** The repeated step closes after six steps.

**Role:** Closure permission.

**Runtime note:** This is native to the first-circle station cycle.

---

## Diagram F.11 - Six Central Cells Around Center

**Placement:** Chapter 7.

**Purpose:** Show the six equal-opening triangles meeting around the center.

**Must show:** Center O, six boundary stations, and six central triangular cells sharing the center.

**Must not show yet:** Coordinate axes, slopes, or algebraic labels.

**Reader takeaway:** Six equal-opening cells fill the turn around the center.

**Role:** Central turn field.

**Runtime note:** This aligns with the runtime cell overlay around the origin.

---

## Diagram F.12 - Hexagon as Six Cells

**Placement:** Chapter 8, with reuse in Chapters 12 and 14.

**Purpose:** Show the hexagon as the outside of six central cells, not as an imported polygon.

**Must show:** Six boundary stations connected as the outside edge, with six triangular cells inside.

**Must not show yet:** General regular-polygon theory or a list of hexagon properties.

**Reader takeaway:** The hexagon is already built by the six equal-opening cells.

**Role:** Source-earned figure and bridge to direction families.

**Runtime note:** This is source-native as the first-circle chord and cell overlay.

---

## Diagram F.13 - Three Direction Families

**Placement:** Chapter 8 or Chapter 9.

**Purpose:** Show that the hexagon sides fall into three same-way pairs.

**Must show:** Three pairs of opposite sides, each pair sharing one visual style or line treatment.

**Must not show yet:** Slope, Cartesian axes, coordinate equations, or parallel-line proof language.

**Reader takeaway:** Direction appears before coordinates.

**Role:** Direction-family permission.

**Runtime note:** This aligns with the three native carrier families: q constant, r constant, and q + r constant.

---

## Diagram F.14 - Triangular Tessellation

**Placement:** Chapter 10.

**Purpose:** Show the field extending beyond the first hexagon into repeated triangular cells.

**Must show:** The original sixfold construction faintly or centrally, with repeated triangular cells extending outward.

**Must not show yet:** Arbitrary tilings, square grids, or coordinate-plane authority.

**Reader takeaway:** The field grows from the first construction.

**Role:** Field-extension permission.

**Runtime note:** This is native to the infinite triangular/barycentric substrate and finite activation layers.

---

## Diagram F.15 - Lawful Transport Across Cells

**Placement:** Chapter 11.

**Purpose:** Show that a relation can move across the field only when its support moves with it.

**Must show:** One marked relation in a source cell, a second cell with the same support, and a clear visual path of lawful transport.

**Must not show yet:** Appearance-only copying, unsupported symmetry claims, or proof-ledger machinery.

**Reader takeaway:** The receipt must travel with the claim.

**Role:** Source-discipline guardrail.

**Runtime note:** This is overlay-derived. It should mark preserved structure, not merely matching shapes.

---

## Diagram F.16 - Rhombus as Two Cells

**Placement:** Chapter 12 or Chapter 14.

**Purpose:** Show a rhombus as two adjacent triangular cells sharing a side.

**Must show:** Two equal-opening triangular cells joined along one shared side and read together as one four-sided figure.

**Must not show yet:** Rhombus formulas, diagonal theorems, or abstract quadrilateral classification.

**Reader takeaway:** A larger figure can be read from cells the field already made.

**Role:** Geometry begins as source-earned figure reading.

**Runtime note:** This is native as a two-cell finite region in the triangular substrate.

---

## Diagram F.17 - Area by Cell Count

**Placement:** Chapter 14.

**Purpose:** Show area beginning as counted triangular cells before formula.

**Must show:** One cell, two cells, six cells, and one larger region counted by cells.

**Must not show yet:** Base times height, square units as the first authority, or formulas for arbitrary regions.

**Reader takeaway:** Area can begin by counting earned cells.

**Role:** Native area seed.

**Runtime note:** This is a finite-region overlay on activated triangular cells.

---

## Diagram F.18 - Right-Meeting Connectors

**Placement:** Chapter 15.

**Purpose:** Show selected connectors in the field meeting right.

**Must show:** The field, selected station-to-station connectors, their meeting point, and the right marker only after the connectors are visible.

**Must not show yet:** Slope perpendicularity, coordinate perpendicularity, or every square-looking corner marked right.

**Reader takeaway:** Rightness is read inside the field. It is not dropped in from outside.

**Role:** Rightness bridge.

**Runtime note:** This is overlay-derived and needs careful connector selection.

---

## Diagram F.19 - First Field-Right Triangle

**Placement:** Chapter 16.

**Purpose:** Show a right triangle extracted from a right meeting inside the field.

**Must show:** A right meeting, two field-supported sides, the side across from the right meeting, and the field faintly behind the triangle.

**Must not show yet:** Sine, cosine, tangent, unit circle, or detached textbook triangle language.

**Reader takeaway:** The right triangle comes from the field.

**Role:** Bridge from rightness to ratio.

**Runtime note:** This depends on the selected right-connector overlay.

---

## Diagram F.20 - 1, sqrt(3), 2 Ratio Packet

**Placement:** Chapter 17.

**Purpose:** Show the first stable right-triangle side packet before trigonometric names.

**Must show:** One field-right triangle with side lengths 1, sqrt(3), and 2, plus the chosen acute angle if needed.

**Must not show yet:** Sine, cosine, tangent names as the first explanation, trig tables, or unit-circle authority.

**Reader takeaway:** The ratios come before the names.

**Role:** Ratio packet.

**Runtime note:** This is derived from the field through rightness and projection. It should carry a short explanatory bridge.

---

## Diagram F.21 - Sine/Cosine/Tangent Side Roles

**Placement:** Chapter 18.

**Purpose:** Name the three side comparisons after the triangle and ratios are visible.

**Must show:** One chosen acute angle, opposite side, adjacent side, and hypotenuse; then the three ratio names.

**Must not show yet:** Identity manipulation, unit-circle-first explanation, or memorized table layout.

**Reader takeaway:** Sine, cosine, and tangent are names for side comparisons.

**Role:** Name-after-picture diagram.

**Runtime note:** This can be a static overlay on the field-right triangle.

---

## Diagram F.22 - Coordinates Added After Construction

**Placement:** Chapter 21.

**Purpose:** Show coordinates arriving after the field is already built.

**Must show:** The constructed field first without coordinates, then coordinate labels added to selected stations.

**Must not show yet:** Coordinates as the source of the field, square-grid substrate, or axes that hide the triangular construction.

**Reader takeaway:** Coordinates record. They do not create.

**Role:** Coordinate audit opening.

**Runtime note:** The runtime may use viewer-native axial coordinates, but the diagram should include an orientation note if manuscript coordinates differ.

---

## Diagram F.23 - Difference Vector and Norm Audit

**Placement:** Chapter 22.

**Purpose:** Show a coordinate difference as a movement record and the norm as a distance audit.

**Must show:** Two stations, their coordinate records, the difference vector between them, and a distance check tied back to the construction.

**Must not show yet:** Coordinate calculation replacing constructional evidence, arbitrary algebraic lengths, or unsupported metric claims.

**Reader takeaway:** The picture builds. The coordinate record checks.

**Role:** Coordinate audit tool.

**Runtime note:** This is native to the axial coordinate substrate and the squared norm q^2 + q*r + r^2.

---

## Diagram F.24 - Algebra as Transformed Relation Memory

**Placement:** Chapter 26, with preparation from Chapters 24 and 25.

**Purpose:** Show one source-earned relation carried through lawful symbolic forms.

**Must show:** A visible relation from the construction, a symbolic record of it, and several equivalent forms connected back to the same source.

**Must not show yet:** Free symbol-shuffling, transformations that change only one side, or formulas detached from figures.

**Reader takeaway:** The form can change. The relation must stay true.

**Role:** Final convergence diagram.

**Runtime note:** This is not a substrate event. It is a symbolic-memory diagram that points back to source-earned relations.

---

## Summary of Derivation Status

| Diagram Range | Status | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| F.3 to F.14 | Mostly source-native | These can be drawn directly from the fixed opening, first circle, stations, steps, cells, closure, carrier families, and field extension. |
| F.15 to F.20 | Overlay-derived | These need selected overlays and careful visual discipline. |
| F.21 | Name-after-picture | This names ratios already seen in the field-right triangle. |
| F.22 to F.23 | Coordinate audit | These use coordinates as records placed after construction. |
| F.1, F.2, F.24 | Reader-map or symbolic-memory | These are not construction events, but they organize or remember what the construction has earned. |

---
# Appendix G — Working Notes
<!-- BEGIN FUTURE_DRAFTING_CONTINUATION_NOTE_V1 -->

## Continuation Note for Future Drafting

Continue by writing one chapter at a time in the agreed voice.

Do not expand source-ledger machinery into the main text.

Do not produce a new full Markdown file unless the user asks for one. Use small PowerShell patch packets that replace named sections in place.

Write for a normal, curious 14-year-old.

Prefer the earliest clear diagram.

Prefer plain action sentences over abstract proof language.

Keep the source references, but do not let them dominate the chapter.

Do not move to a later branch too quickly. Each branch must feel like it grows from the same visible construction.

When in doubt, ask:

1. Where can the reader first see this?
2. What is the simplest sentence that matches the picture?
3. What name can wait until after the reader has seen the thing?
4. What claim has not yet been earned?

The goal is weightless illustration with source discipline underneath.

<!-- END FUTURE_DRAFTING_CONTINUATION_NOTE_V1 -->

Use this section for drafting decisions, cuts, unresolved dependencies, and chapter-level editorial notes.

# Compilation Notes

This is a flat Markdown scaffold.

No repository structure is assumed.  
No folders are required.  
All content can remain in this single file until the book is ready for extraction, layout, or compilation.

Suggested later export formats:

- PDF
- HTML
- DOCX
- EPUB
